SAMOAN REBELS
LOSE HEAVILY
Fearful Execution During the Shell- j
ing of Vailima. I
AUCKLAND. N. Z., May 4.â Advices I
received here from Apia, Samoa, to
April 25 state that the rebel loss by the |
shelling of the Mataafan stronghold at '
Vailima by the warships of Great Brit
ain and the United States was quite
heavy. Many of the rebels were
wounded. The house in which the late
Robert Louis Stevenson lived was rid- I
died with shot.
On April 22 there was a sharp skir
mish between rebels and Lieutenant R.
A. Gaunt's brigade near the town of
Vailima. In this engagement one Ma
taafan was killed. The lines of the
.friendlies were attacked in the dark
ness at two points, but none of them
was killed. On April 23 there was 1
some bush fighting. Lieutenant Gaunt's
brigade^ numbering 900 men, advanced j
under _ hot fire. The enemy lost sev
eral men, the bodies of the dead being
dragged away. Of the friendlies one i
was killed and three were wounded. The .
British lieutenants, Innes, Hickman,
Shuler and Heathcote, under Lieuten
ant Gaunt, led the loyalists.
The New Zealand Government i
steamer Tutanekai had arrived at j
Apia with dispatches announcing thej
request of the international commis- J
sioners that the inhabitants peacefully
await the arrival of the commissioners, i
who are now on their way to the islands I
from San Francisco. A meeting of the'
three Consuls, in view of this message,
was held, but without result, the Ger
man Consul dissenting from the propo
sitions of the other two Consuls. .'*_ >~~
Admiral Albert Kautz, U. S. N. and
Captain Stuart of the British cruiser
Tauranga dispatched a message
through a French priest to Ma
taafa, ordering him to withdraw his j
forces outside Of a line ten miles long
and six miles broad. Mataafa replied
defiantly, refusing to withdraw unless
the German Consul and the captain of
the German cruiser Falke agreed. Ad
miral Kautz and Captain Stuart then
sent an ultimatum to Mataafa, threat
ening to compel th*- withdrawal of his
forces. In support of this ultimatum a
plan of action was decided upon. Lieu
tenant Gaunt's brigade was strength
ened and a heavy gun was landed at
Mulinuu and war rockets were got in â¢
readiness. Being informed of these
prepositions Mataafa made prompt re
ply, agreeing to withdraw immediately.
On April 25 an important meeting of
the King and his chiefs was held. They
were informed of the action that had
been taken by the British and Ameri- '
can naval commanders and gave it
general approval.
Lieutenant Gaunt's brigade is now
patrolling the prescribed area. King
Tanu visited Admiral Kautz and Cap
tain Stuart on board the American and
British ships, and was received with
the customary salutes on his arrival
and departure. Hufnagel. manager of
the plantation upon which the attack |
was made on British and American
sailors, resulting in the death of Lieu
tenant Freeman, of the British naw.
Lieutenant Lam lal and Ensign John
R. Monaghan, of the United States
cruiser Philadelphia, and two others,
is still detained on board the German
cruiser Falke.
The gun carriage from the Philadel
phia, which was lost at the time of
the engagement, has been recovered.

THE FILIPINOS MAKE
DESPERATE RESISTANCE
Church at Caloocan, Ruined by Fire From American Guns.

BEFORE THE
AMERICANS
Brave Colonel Funston Was
Slightly Wounded in At
tack Upon San Tomas,
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MANILA, May 5, 8:25 a. m.
â In spite of the peaceful
overtures of their commissioners,
the Filipinos vigorously resisted
the advance of General Mac-
Arthur's division from Apalit
toward San Fernando, righting
desperately at long range after
running from trench to trench
when driven out by the Ameri
can artillery.
The movement upon San Tomas com
menced at 5:30 o'clock in the morning.
General Hale's brigade, consisting of
five Oatlings, under command of Ma
jor Young of the Sixth Artillery, two
battalions of the Fifty-first lowa regi
ment, the First Nebraska and the First
Dakota regiment, advanced along the
road a few miles west of the railway
line.
General Wheaton, with Hotchkiss and
Catlings, under the command of Lieu
tenant Naylor of the Utah Light Ar
tillery, mounted on handcars, pushed
ahead, the Twentieth Kansas and First
Montana regiments deploying to the
right and left when feasible.
The .try to be traversed proved
the worst yet encountered, mil'-.- of
formidable marshes and many unford
\ able streams delaying the advance ma
! terially.
Both brigades met with resistance =.-
I multaneously on approaching the river
! near San Tomas, which is about eight
j kilometers from Apalit. The center
j span of the railroad bridge had drop
j ped into the river and the rebels had
I only left a small force to check Gen
i eral Wheaton, their main body lying in.
strong trenches in front of General
Hale.
Although the attacking force poured
a very heavy artillery and musketry
j fire across the river the enemy stub
bornly resisted for over an hour, ulti
j mately breaking when Major Young
shelled their left flank and then re
i treating along the river bank under
I cover.
Genera] Wheaton, in the meantime,
I tried Ineffectually to draw the fire of
the Filipinos In the trenches east of
: the track.
As soon as they discovered that the
j nature of the country would permit
only a few skirmishes on each side of
the embankment the rebels regained
their courage and fought desperately
for three-quarters of an hour in the
face of .the American volleys and a
rapid-fire fusillade until outflanked by
the Montana regiment. Then a general '
scramble ensued, most of the enemy
boarding trains that were in readiness
and the others taking the road to San
Fernando, after burning the villages of
San Tomas and Monalin. About noon
General Wheaton crossed the broken
bridge, cleared the stragglers out of
the villages and advanced toward San
Fernando. General Hale effected a.
crossing simultaneously, after a slight
delay necessary to repair a stone
bridge.
Our loss up to that hour (noon) was j
two members of the Nebraska regiment
and one of the Montana killed, and
Captain Albrecht, three members of
the Kansas regiment, two of the Mon
tana, four of the Nebraska and one of
the South Dakota wounded. The ene
my's loss was very slight.
After a short rest the advance was '
1 continued, General Wheaton encoun- j
SAN FRANCISCO, FRIDAY, MAY" 5, 1899.
tering the flrst series of intrenchments
near San Fernando. The rebels now
opened a hot fire.
Colonel Funston of the Twentieth
Kansas was wounded; one lieutenant
was killed and four wounded while
leading four companies of the Kansas
regiment to outflank the enemy.
General Hale pushed along the road,
flanking the trenches. More than a
hundred sick and wounded men from
General Lawton's brigade were
brought to Manila from Malolos by
last night's train. The heat is unbear
able.
The Filipinos retreated toward San
Isidro, and it is expected that they will
make a stand at Arayat, at which
place the whole of the rebel forces in
the province of Pampangas is con
centrating.
It is reported that, impressed by re
cent events, the Filipinos are wavering
in their allegiance to the insurrection
and are likely to assume a neutral at
titude.
It seems that General Antonio Luna's
forces are destined to destruction with
in a few days unless they surrender or
scatter. The American army has been
skillfully posted at points of iir.mer.se
strategic advantage. The insurgents
expected them to advance on Balinag
by way of Quingua, and had furrowed
the entire country beyond Quingua
with the strongest sort of intrench
ments. Instead of taking this course
General Mac Arthur swung toward San
Tomas, the route to which as almost
unprotected. and General Lawton
brought his brigade in against Balsuam
from the southeast, covering the line
of retreat of the rebels toward the
mountains and depriving them of a
refuge upon which they had already
counted.
Genera*! Lawton captured 50,000
bushels of rice, an important part of
the stores established by the rebels.
The Spanish commissioners have
made another futile attempt for the re
lief of the Spaniards held as prisoners
by the Filipinos. Their vessels steamed
to the port nearest Dagupan. Fearing
to land they sent a letter to Aguinaldo
saying they were authorized to ex
change 15,000 Filipinos for the Spanish
prisoners. The reply was received from
Senor Mabini. dated May 2, saying that
Aguinaldo was negotiating for peace,
pending which negotiations he could
not discuss the question of exchange of
prisoners.
AN ENGLISH VERSION
PROBABLY OVERDRAWN
VICTORIA. B. C, May 4.â Two Aus
tralian newspaper men. who arrived
here last night from the Orient by
the steamer Rio Jun Maruâ J. Rus
sell and G. S. Wittonâ gave the Times
the following account of their impres
sions of the American soldiers, their
present adversaries, and of the treat
ment meted out by the Americans tc
.the Filipinos: â¢
"The Americans,". Mr. Russell said,
"are treating the insurgents in a most
shameful manner. The sentries in
halting the unfortunates, do not warn
them. The general practice seems to
be to 'fire and then sing out.' "
A mistake was made during Mr. Rus
sell's stay which nearly cost an Ameri
can officer his lite. A captain, Mr. Rus
sell could not remember his name or
regiment, went out to his pickets and,
pursuant to the practice of firing and
then warning the comer, a sentry fired
at him as he approached in the gloom
and blew his chin off. Another step
and the unfortunate officer would have
been killed outright. _
"You never hear of these little Inci
dents over here," said Mr. Russell; "the
press censor goes through everything
the correspondents wish to send out
and the blue pencil obliterates all that
kind of thing. The Americans as a
rule have no love for the Filipinos and
if they had their way would summarily
shoot their captives. Of course there
is considerable to be said in excuse
of this bloodthirstiness. Several in
stances have been witnessed when a
band of insurgents came and parleyed
under a flag of truce with the Ameri
cans an/1 then treacherously hurled
spears at unarmed soldiers. But, all
things considered, it cannot be said
that the Filipinos are being treated
properly in the fighting. The Ameri
cans used Aguinaldo as a decoy when
the war was on with Spain, when his
trained. fighters who had so long fought
the Spaniards were of use to them and
then, to use the picturesque vernacular
of the United States soldiers, 'they
turn him down.' "
As for* the treatment of prisoners by
United States troops the following in
cident came under the ken of Mr. Rus
sell:
A reconnoitering party found three
insurgents just within the city trying
to creep out past the trenches and
seized them. One of the trio, a young,
and sturdy looking native, seemed to
be practically bleeding to death from
his wounds. The three were hurried
to the guardhouse and violently thrown
to the floor. Then their arms were
wrenched from them and they were
kicked around like curs. The prison
ers, expectant of instant death, com
posedly struggled to their knees and
chanted what seemed to be a prayer,
and then, springing up. bared their
bosoms and waited for the expected
blow. But it did not come. They were
hurled back into the guardhouse and
then they passed from the visitor's
view.
The soldiers of Uncle Sam in the
Philippines, according to Mr. Russell,
are homesick, and are thoroughly tired
of the' whole business. ... .
"Mark my words," said Mr. Russell,
"the United States has a bigger con
tract in the Philippines than they
thought they had and are liable to get
Into a hole before they get through
with it. The soldiers would welcome
any way that would give them a chance
to throw oft" their burden."
CROKER SAYS SELL
THE PHILIPPINES
LONDON, May 4.â 'What is the lat
est news the Herald has received from
the Philippines?" Richard Croker
asked, on the arrival of the steamship
New York at Southampton this morn
ing, and when I told him that negotia
tions for a cessation of hostilities were
still in progress, with some promise of
successful conclusion, he exclaimed
emphatically:
"Good; I am glad to hear that: but
we ought to get rid of the islands.
Spain did a good stroke of business for
herself in obtaining twenty millions
from the United States for them. They
were a continual drain on her. and I
think that we should either sell or
trade our rights in the island:, after
peace and order are restored. I do not
believe we ought to give away the
possessions the war brought us without
receiving something in return.
"I consider the scheme advocated by
the Herald for the exchange of the
Philippines for the British West Indies
a very good one, but England does not
appear to think it would be a good
trade for her. She has enough on her
hands in Africa. India and the Far
East without taking on more trouble
in the shape of the Philippines.
* "If we retain the islands and attempt
to govern them from a distance, the
question will figure very prominently
in the next Presidential election."

DR. SAMUEL KENNEDY
MAY ESCAPE DEATH
English Convict Confesses to the
Murder of Dolly Reynolds, for
Which Kennedy Has Been
Condemned.
NEW TORK. May 4.â A special to the
World from Washington says: Dr. Sam
uel J. Kennedy, now under sentence of
death in Sing Sing Prison for the murder
of Dolly Reynolds in the Grand Hotel
last August, may yet escape the electric
chair. Richard T. Nicholson, now con
fined in Wormwood Scrubbs Prison. Lon
don, has made an alleged confession of
the crime, acknowledging that he mur
dered Miss Reynolds after a quarrel in
the bedroom where she was found. This
confession Nicholson sent to the prison
authorities. They referred It through va
rious official channels to the Marquis of
Salisbury, the Prime Minister. From his
office it was sent to Sir Julian Paunce
fote. thence to Secretary Hay, who will
send it, with other papers, to Governor
Roosevelt. It is expected^ the Governor
will grant a respite pending a full in
vestigation. Nicholson is undergoing a
sentence of eighteen months for misde
meanor.

EXTRA SESSION
NOT LIKELY
TO BE CALLED
The President Would Like
to Pass the Currency
Reform Bill.
â â¦ â
TROUBLES IN THE WAY
â .Â» â
Congress Might Worry Him Before

Special Dispatch to The Call
NEW YORK, May 4.â The Washing
ton correspondent of the Herald tele
graphs: Since it was announced that
the (Caucus Committee of Republican
representatives had agreed at Atlantic
City on a currency reform bill, which
is to be made a party measure if it
meets the approval of the Republican
members of the Senate Finance Com
mittee, there has been a revival of the
report that the President will call an
extra session of Congress. It is urged
that it would be good policy to have
an extra session, so as to get the House
organized promptly and have the cur
rency bill passed in o. ier that the
transition from the old system to new
might take place before the beginning
of the Presidential campaign.
It cannot be learned, however, that
the President has any intention at the
present time of calling an extra ses
sion. I am assured by party leaders
with wltom he has discussed the situ
ation that the President wishes to have
all the time possible before the meet
ing of Congress to restore order in the
Philippines and to decide upon his pol
icy for the future government of those
islands.
While it is recognized that It would
be well to have the new currency law
In operation before the campaign
opens, the disposition of the Republi
can leaders seems to be to try to get
it up and pass it in regular session
rather than to take it up in an extra
session.
The details of the agreement reached
by the Caucus Committee are carefully
guarded from the public on the theory
that It would be discourteous to mem
bers of the Senate Finance Committee
for the House committee to announce
its conclusions before submitting them
to the Senate. There is reason to be
lieve, however, that the committee
found It hard to reach any agreement
at all, and that the measure agreed
upon falls far short of being a com
prehensive scheme of currency and
banking reform.

REPUBLICANS
OPPOSED TO
ALL TRUSTS
The Party Will So Declare
! in Its Next National
Platform.
â â¢ â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, May 4.â The Herald's
Washington correspondent sends the
following: Senator-elect Depews de
claration that the next .Republican
platform will undoubtedly contain a
declaration condemning trusts Is con
curred in by many Republican leaders.
- Representative Henderson of lowa,
who. If he Is not elected Speaker of the
House, will be chairman of the Judi
ciary Committee, to which any anti
trust bill would be referred, said to me
to-night that he had no doubt the Re
publican party would declare against
trusts in its next platform. He said
aggregations of capital engaged in va
rious industries were becoming so
enormous that they could not be ig
nored. Â»
A high official of the administration,
in discussing the trust question, said to
.me: "There is no doubt the platforms
of both great political parties will con
tain anti-trust declarations. I believe
the Democrats will make opposition to
trusts one of the principal planks in
their platform. They will denounce the
Republican party as friendly to trusts.
We will be ready to meet them on that
ground, however, and we will show by
the record of the party in the present
administration that everything possible
under the law has been done to re
strain trusts.
"It will be recognized that suits can
be brought when there is some proba
bility that the Government will win
them. We could go ahead and file suits
against every trust in the country, but
it would be pure demagogy to file suits
which the Attorney General would
know in advance would be thrown out
by the court, in view of its previous
decisions."

WELBURN IN
THE CLUTCH
OF THE LAW
Fugitive Ex Revenue Collector
Captured After Many
Months.
HE WAS MASQUERADING
AS A PRIVATE SOLDIER
En Route With His Company for the Philip
pines When the Federal Officers Found
Him at the Presidio Reser
vation.
OSCA M. WELBURN, ex-Collec- '
tor of Internal Revenue for the <
Northern District of California, '
for whom the Federal authorities ,
have been looking ever since his :
mysterious disappearance more than a
year ago, was arrested yesterday at ,
the Presidio, where he was shunning
publicity in the garb of a private in
I Company, Ninth Infantry. U. S. A.,
en route to the Philippines. He is now
in the County Jail, to* await trial on an j
indictment of thirty-one counts charg- I
ing him with failure, while acting as i
disbursing agent for the Government,
to turn over moneys collected by him. ,
The arrest of Welburn, under the ;
circumstances, was as complete a sur
prise to the authorities as it must have
been to the fugitive. After his sudden !
disappearance in December, 1597, the
Government seemed to be unable to [
find any trace of him. He had dropped I
completely "off the map" and what few !
efforts were made to overhaul him were j
looked upon as purely perfunctory.
From time to time rumors to the effect i
that he was in Mexico, in Texas and
Alaska were circulated, but he managed
to escape arrest, and had he not had the
hardihood to enlist, knowing as he must
that his regiment was likely at any
time to be ordered to San Francisco,
it Is not so certain that he would be
now occupying a prison cell.
Welburn, or as he is pleased to style
himself in the army. Olan H. Bedell, ar
rived in' the city last Friday night in
i company with others of a detachment of
thirty recruits sent here from Camp Haw
ley. Galveston, Tex., where he enlisted
April 22 last. The detachment stopped
the first night in town at the Interna
tional Hotel on Kearny street, and on the
next day, undoubtedly through Welburn's
curiosity to learn how things had been
going on at the Federal building since
his hasty departure, his identity was re
vealed to one who put the United States
Marshal on his track. This person was
William E. Davis, a colored man, whose
express wagon stood at the Appraiser's
building during all of the time that Wel
burn was in office.
Davis happened to be at the Interna
tional Hotel Saturday morning, when he
was approached by Welburn, who felt
sure that his closest friend would be un
able to recognize in the clean-shaven,
sun-browned, stalwart, blue-uniformed
Olan H. Bedell the ex-Collector of
Revenue wanted by Uncle Sam. That is
where he made his mistake. Davis had
not been talking to him more than a min
ute or two when the impression grew
strong on him that the infantryman was
none other than his former patron, Osca
M. Welburn. \
Davis lost no time in conveying this im
pression to the proper authorities. He
hurried to Bert H. : Thomas, special
revenue collector, and told him what he
knew. He said he could not be mistaken
ln the strongly marked features of the
former Collector. Thomas, in trun, re
ported the matter to United States Mar
shal Shine, who began the search for th.
man. Shine took with him to the Presidio
on Monday last Deputy Mofiltt because of
his acquaintance with Welburn. The two
officers were unable to locate their man
on Monday and met with no better suc
cess on Tuesday and Wednesday. They
were greatly Impeded In their search by
reason of the fact that there are about
1000 recruits at the Presidio, and at that
time they did not know the name under
which Welburn had enlisted. Finally
Marshal Shine went to Captain Hardin,
who* has command of the casual detach
ment, for assistance, and that officer sug
gested to him that he look over the de
scriptive lists of the recruits who had re
ported on Saturday, for that was the day
that the detachment which had stopped
at the International Hotel went out to
the Presidio. *,' '._]'
When Shine read the descriptive list of
Bedell he declared his belief that Bedell
was the man they wanted. This was yes
terday. Bedell had been assigned to Com
pany I. of the casual detachment, pend
ing assignment to some regular regiment,

While it would be Impossible to make a
mistake as to the identity of the prisoner,
once attention was called to him. those
who saw Welburn yesterday could not
help noticing the wonderful change that
had come over. the manner of the man
they once knew so well. He Is almost of
another mold, only the strongly marked
features being unmistakable, notwith
standing the fact that they are minus the
heard that graced the revenue collector
and that they are darkly tanned from ex
posure in a military camp. The blue eye
is unmistakable, as well as the long

marked Welburn as ma' years older
than the "Bedell" he claimed to be are
not to be concealed by the close hair crop
of the soldier. The life in camp has also
made a shapelier figure than that which
was so well known in the tenderloin in
Ihe halcyon days of Welburn's greatness.
It is above anything the figure of the sol
dier, erect, well knit, muscular and ac
tive, with all the gra*ce that mes from
attention to drill. The man looks many
years younger than he did in the days
of his trouble, but no one would say that |
he was as young as he entered himself in !
the army. He shows a number of years
more hat 33.
What Welburn has been doing with
himself is probably only known to him

with which he had til? note conveyed to
the address of hi.- son it is more than
likely that the members of his family
have heard from time to time of his wan

The fact that he enlisted is taken as

sical condition shows also that he has

fall. He shows all the evidence of having
led a sober life for months. He will need
the constitution he nas nursed since his
flight to enrry him through the trial he
will have to face.
The story of Welburn's downfall Is fa
miliar to every newspaper reader in the
State. The exposure came with t he sui
cide of Isaac Norton, who was his cashier
and who left .-> note saying that he was
short In his accounts to the extent of
many thousand dollars. This led to an
Investigation of the internal revenue of
fice and the subsequent indictment of
Welburn for embezzlement. He disap
peared before a warrant could be served
upon him and was not found for several
days afterward. He was brought to trial
and aft. many days a jury in the United
States District Court October 15, 1897,
brought in a verdict, of "not guilty."
Welburn was discharged, and while there
were rumoi*. that he would be indicted
for other alleged misdeeds in office, no
move was made against him. He must
have been satisfied, however, that danger
menaced, â¢ for he disappeared about
Christmas time of that year. Some days
after his flight the United States Grand
Jury brought in the indictment under
which he is now held.
Welburn's bondsmen were Daniel M.
Burns ar.d Thomas H. Williams, who had
gone surety for him In the sum of $40.
--000. Suit was begun against them some
time ago to recover the shortage in the
office made by the defalcation of Isaac
Norton. That suit has not yet come to
trial.
Welburn will be taken before United
States Commissioner Heacock this morn
ing, when the date of his preliminary
hearing will be set. The prisoner will
probably be represented by Frank H.
Gould, who defended him in his former
trouble.

ON THE VERGE
OF STARVATION
Two Hundred Klondik
ers in Deadly Peril.
â â¦ â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
VICTORIA, B. C, May 4.â Unless
government or private aid is immedi
ately forthcoming for the last of the
deluded unfortunates who sought to
reach the Klondike by the miscalled
trail from Ashcroft, death will shortly
reap an abundant harvest at Dease
Lake and Liard post, where the 200 or
more surviving miners are making their
last stand against starvation.
So desperate is their position that
Alexander S. Brindle, the mall carrier
and Postmaster at Telegraph Creek,
and Andrew McGregor, a courageous
surveyor, took their lives in their hands
to bring out the distressing news, mak
ing their way down the Stikine with a
team of dogs when the Indians would
not venture on the crumbling, water
fnced ice. nd declared it suicide to at
tempt the trip.
The Information that Brindle gives is
that fully 200 men are confronting the
specter famine in the Dease Lake camp,
with not a dollar among them and their
supplies exhausted. The greater num
ber of the skeleton-like horses have
been killed and eaten, and when Brin
iile and McGregor started out the pack
mules were furnishing food for the
cheerless camp. This fare is varied
only by the occasional trapping of some
if the curs straying beyond the con
fines of the Indian villages.
A Hudson Bay post is 110 miles from
the scene of distress, and the request is
made that the Government instruct Its
management to relieve the distress.
The agent of this post, through his su- j
perior officer at Glenora, has furnished
the following partial list of the starv
ing prospectors at Francis Lake, Liard !

DENVER HAS SECURED
THE BIG FISTIC MILL
DENVER. Colo., May Manager Otto
C. Floto of the Colorado Athletic Associa
tion to-night received a telegram from
Brady and Julian, representing Jeffries
and Fitzsimmons, accepting his offer and
agreeing to fight in Denver. Mr. Floto
was in telegraphic communication with
the managers of the two lighters all day,
and finally secured their acceptance of his
offer, which includes a purse of $25,000.
The other details of the agreement were
not made public, but It was announced
that articles of agreement were mailed
to Denver to-night. Manager Floto stated
to-night that work will be begun at once
on an amphitheater capable of seating
15.000 people. The date or the meeting hsts
not yet been agreed upon.

LANGLEY'S NEW
FLYING MACHINE
Government Expert Has
It About Perfected.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK. May 4.â A Washington
special to the World says: Professor S.
P. Langley, secretary of the Smithson
ian Institution and Government expert
on aerial navigation, will booh make a
trial experiment with his new flying
machine. This airship has been con
structed with the $25,000 appropriated
by the last Congress for the purpose of
experimenting with and developing fly
ing machines under the direction of the
War Department.
Professor Langley's new machine is
being built at Quantico, Va., a short
distance from Washington, on the Po
tomac River. He is working along the
same line in which he has achieved
much success in the past and has de
veloped a machine which has demon
strated its ability to navigate the air.
Professor Langley calls his machine an
aerodrome and it is purely a "flying
machine," that is. It is something much
heavier than air and entirely different
in principle from a balloon, which floats
only on air of its lightness, as a ship
does in water.
It is patterned after nature's flying
machines, birds, and only differs in
principle of action in that Instead of
being propelled by wings it gains and
maintains headway through the me
dium of a propeller fashioned on the
i order of a ship's screw.
Professor Langley has chartered the
steamer Bartholdi and will visit the
place where his ship is building within
the next few days. The experiments
will continue until the machine reaches
such a stage of perfection that it can
be publicly exhibited and inspected by
officers of the War Department. The
machine is ready now for its trial trips.
A CHICAGO WOMAN
KILLS HER MOTHER
Says She Was Driven to the Deed by
the Elder Woman's
Act.
CHICAGO, . May 4.â Mrs. Augustus
Styles, after waiting two hours In a dark
recess of a hallway at the entrance to
Wallatt's Hall, Burlington street and
North avenue, this afternoon, shot and
killed her mother, Mrs. Catherine Schultz.
Mrs. Schultz was to attend a meeting of
the Martha Washington Prauen Verein,
which was held in the hall, and the
daughter lay In wait for her until she
came. Five shots were fired, three taking
effect.
Mrs. Styles, who is 35 years old. said
she was driven to the act by her mother
revealing a closed chapter of her early
life to Mrs. Styles' daughter, 16 years of
age. The child's parents were not regu
larly married, and Mrs. Schultz is said
to have disclosed this fact to her grand
child. The matricide has been arrested.
UNSATISFACTORY DECISION.
Omaha Kid Given the Verdict Over
Tommy Hogan of New York.
â¢ LOUISVILLE. Ky.. May 4.â Before a
house packed to the doors Oscar Gard- !
ncr, the renowned Omaha Kid, received
the decision over Tommy Hogan, the
crack New York feather-weight, to-night
in a 20-round bout given under the aus
pices of the Nonpareil Athletic Club.
The fighting was terrific from start to
finish. Gardner paid attention chiefly to
Hogan's stomach, and landed blows that
would have settled matters quickly with
a man less finely trained than the New
York lad. Hogan opened up an old wound
over Gardner's right eye, and the Kid
went to his corner time and again with
the blood pouring from his damaged op
tic. In the eighteenth round Hogan
drove his right on to Gardner's chin, the
bell saving Gardner from a knockout.
The decision of the referee, was not sanc
tioned by the majority of the spectators,
they believing that the contest should
have been declared a draw.
McXINLEY'S VACATION.
The President Going to Virginia Hot
Springs Next Week.
WASHINGTON, May 4.â President Mc-
Kinley decided to leave the city next
Monday, to remain away for two and pos
sibly three weeks. According to his pres
ent intention the President will spend his
vacation at Hot Springs, Va., as he is
suffering from a slight attack of rheuma
tism, and is feeling th.- strain that has
been on him for many months. He is anx
ious to enjoy perfect rest and at the same
time to avail himself of hot baths, which
will remove the rheumatic tendency. The
President and his wife will be domiciled
at the Homestead Hotel, as none of the
cottages at the springs are ready for oc
cupancy. The hot springs are only eight
hours' railroad ride from Washington, so
that the President could return at once
to the capital if any occasion arises fur
his presence here. .During his absence
the -id, 'at will transact only such busi
ness as absolutely requires his personal
attention.
PACIFIC CABLE SCHEME.
London Papers Blame the Govern-
ment for Its Dilatoriness.
LONDON, May '.âThe report of the Pa
chic Cable Committee, which was issued

Lineman's Fatal Fall.
SACRAMENTO, May 4.-Charles Dur
kec. a lineman in the employ of the Capi
tal Telephone Company, met with an ac
cident to-day which will probably result
fatally. He had ascended a pole to string
a wire. The pole had rotted off at the
base and his weight toppled it over. In
falling his neck struck a wire guy, from
which he rebounded and fell to the
ground. It is believed by attending phy
sicians that his spine is dislocated and he
cannot recover. : _v

FIERCE FIGHT
WITH BURGLARS
San Jose Man Puts Two
Robbers to Flight.
. c
ONE OF THEM BADLY HURT
â â¦- â
HIT ON THE HEAD WITH A
WATER PITCHER.
â¦
Made His Escape With the Assistance
of His Companion, but Left
a Broad Trail of
Blood.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE, May Architect F. D.
Wolfe, who resides at 651 South Sixth
street, had an exciting encounter with
burglars at his home about 2:30 o'clock
this morning. He broke a pitcher and
washbowl over a man's head and then
threw him clown a flight of stairs. Evi
dences of the struggle were plainly
seen this morning. The hall and stair
way were covered with broken crockery
and blood. Wounds were evidently in
flicted that may lead to the burglar's
capture.
The burglar was no less a personage
than "the borer," whose work in San
Jose for months has baffled the police
and terrorized timed women. His title
is derived from his effecting an en
trance into houses by cutting out
panels in doors with an auger.
Last night he had a partner with
him. A hole about four inches square
had been bored out of the front door.
Wolfe was awakened by some noise
and saw a man standing in the door
of the bedroom with a lighted candle
In his hand. The man shoved the
candle in his pocket and started down
stairs. Wolfe jumped out of bed and
started in pursuit. At the head of the
stairs he met another burglar coming
from a rear room. Wolfe grabbed a
water pitcher and broke it over the
fellow's head. This staggered him and
Wolfe followed up his advantage by
using the washbowl. The fellow reeled
like a drunken man and Wolfe then
threw the man down stairs. In falling
he broke the newel post at the bottom.
The burglar made his way out and dis
appeared. After daylight the man's
tracks were followed by means of blood
spots along the sidewalk. A couple of
blocks from the house it was evident
from the footprints that his companion
had joined him and helped him along
: The police followed the trail of blood
i down Reed street to Market and then
: along Market street to the center of
: town.
This afternoon drops of blood were
found leading out Julian street to
Coyote Creek, and a large number of
officers made a thorough search of the
creekbed. It is thought the man took
refuge there.
The lower part of Wolfe's house had
been ransacked, but nothing was taken.
Some silverware was passed by.
"The borer" has been operating in
San Jose for four months. In that time
no less than fifty houses have been en
tered. But little has been taken.

MUST DIE ON
THE GALLOWS
Rev. Ceo. E. Morrison's
Sentence Affirmed.
ACTION OF TEXAS COURTS
â â¦- â
CONDEMNED MAN CONVICTED OF
POISONING HIS WIFE.
â¦
Formerly Lived in This State, Wher
He Married the Woman He
Murderedâ Cold-Blooded
Crime.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
AUSTIN, Tex.. May 4.â The Court of
Criminal Appeals here to-day affirmed
the death sentence of Rev. George E.
Morrison, who murdered his wife a few
months ago at Panhandle City, Texas,
by strychnine poison.
This is one of the most remarkable
murder cases in the criminal annals of
Texas. Rev. Mr. Morrison was born and
reared in Massachusetts, and his father
is a presiding elder of the Methodist
church in California. The condemned
man lived for some time in that State
and was married there to the woman
he afterward poisoned. They removed
to Panhandle City about four years ago
and Rev. Mr. Morrison was placed in
charge of the Methodist congregation.
He was held in high esteem by the peo
ple and his domestic life was apparent
ly happy.
A year or more ago he went to Kan
sas City, Mo., to undergo medical treat
ment. While there he met a young lady
who was one of his schoolmates in his
boyhood days, and to whom he had
been engaged before leaving his Mas
sachusetts home. The spark of love
was rekindled in the breast of the
Texas minister and without telling the
woman of his wife in Texas he began
paying her ardent attention. He told
her he was a wealthy cattleman and
had a large ranch in Texas. He re
turned to his wife in Panhandle City
in the course of a few weeks and pro
ceeded to carry on a correspondence
with his sweetheart, mailing his letters
from a neighboring town.
It is alleged in the statement of facts
in the case that the convicted man
carefully planned the murder. He
waited until the physicians in the town
were temporarily absent and then ad
ministered the fatal drug. Immediately
after the funeral Morrison left for
Kansas City. Suspicions were aroused
and the body was disinterred and an
analysis of the stomach revealed that
death was due to strychnine poison.
Morrison heard of the proceedings and
fled from Kansas City. He was pur
sued through several Western States
and Mexico and finally retraced to
Kansas City, where his arrest occurred.
On his person was found forged deed*?
to large ranch properties in Texas,
which he had used to make his intended
in Kansas City believe he was a man
of wealth. When his trial came off the
man's father wrote to the court offi
cials declining to aid his son for the
expressed reason that he believed him
guilty. The date of the hanging will
be set when the criminal court mandate
reaches the trial court.
ROHDER PREFERRED DEATH.
Henry Rohder. a canvasser for the sale
of bottled beer for J. H. Hall, agent for
the Fredericksburg Brewery, committed
suicide yesterday afternoon by shooting
himself with a pistol in the temple.
Rohder was driving Â«a bottled beer
wagon for Hall, and stopped at Barney
Farley's old resort on the Ingleside road
to solicit orders, as was his custom. He
was drunk, and when he asked the bar
tender whether he wanted any bottled
beer the latter replied that he might bring
in a case if he had not drunk it all him
self. Rohder, with the half-maudlin re
mark, "Lots of beer," stepped into Do
toilet and placing a pistol to his right
temple discharged it and fell dead upon
the floor.
His employer says that P.ohder had a
quarrel with his wife the evening previ
ous and went into the barn and slept
there. The dead man resided with his
wife and two children at 1539 Treat ave
nue, and was about 40 years old.

LOST THE GEMS
AND THE GIRL
Why a Swell Wedding
Was Abandoned.
â 
â  â â¦ â â 
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PASADENA, May 4.â About two years
ago Miss Jessie Swope Staines met Sam
uel Healey Gilbert. A year ago they be
came engaged to be married. Young Gil
bert was only 19 and he had little or no
money, but his father had promised him
that when he desired to marry he would
give him a start in life. So when Gilbert
decided that Miss Staines, the pretty
daughter of Mr. and Mrs. Frederick
Bremba Staines, society people, living at
1070 South Orange Grove avenue, Pasade
na's swellest street, was the girl for him,
he gave her a handsome ring.
Gilbert went East a short time ago to
arrange for his marriage, which was to
take place Wednesday. April 26, at All
Saints' Episcopal Church, in this city. Mr.
and Mrs. Staines bad the invitations
printed and sent to their friends. Young
Gilbert, upon going to Evansville. Ind.,
found that his father had failed and
could not provide for his son.
Gilbert was at his wit's end. He saw
his bride slipping away from him; he
knew the invitations to the wedding were
in the hands of the printer. He had no
money, but he had an aunt, and the aunt
had some valuable diamonds. Gilbert was
tempted, yielded, took the diamond! and
set forth for Pasadena to be married. But
the theft was discovered and detectives
set out hotfoot for the diamond thief.
Gilbert was arrested at St. Louis and
confessed. But the family, fearing dis
grace, offered to let him off if he restored
the diamonds. This he did. His friends
came to his aid and he kept on to Pasa
dena, hoping to get here before the wed
ding invitations were issued; hoping too.
perhaps, to continue bis engagement even
If the wedding was postponed. But the
girl's father refused to permit his daugh
ter to marry the young man. The girl
abides by her father's decision and GU
bert has returned home single.
THE ENGAGEMENT
PLEASES BOTH SIDES
Washington Society All Agog Over
the Betrothal of Julia Dent
Grant.
WASHINGTON, May Much interest
j is manifested in Washington society over :
the engagement of Miss Julia Dent Grant,
j daughter of General and Mrs. Fred D
j Grant, to Prince Michel Cantacuzene of
I the Russian Imperial Guards. The name
] of Cantacuzene is one of the oldest in
'â  Russia and dates from the time of the I
Byzantine empire, when Constantinople
was the capital. The name is now a ]
i familiar one in Russia and Roumania.
! The father of the young Prince was di- :
rector of foreign religions to the Minister
of the Interior, as all faiths outside of
the Greek churchâ the established church !
of Russiaâ are included In the adminis
tration of the Interior Department.
Prince Cantacuzene, who has also the
title of Count Speranskiy, Is related on the
maternal side to the late Prince Gorts- j
chakoff, who a quarter of a century ago !
was the "Grand Old Man' of Russia,
being Grand Chancellor of Russia and
Minister of Foreign Relations. The fact
that the Cantacuxenes own an estate near
Moscow does not signify that they are
possessed of great wealth, for there is
one family in that country of vast domain
which owns 2,500,000 acres and is not re
garded as rich. The youthful Prince .is I
said to be unusually talented and dis
tinguished. Russians in Washington are
particularly pleased over the alliance, as
they regard Miss Grant as not only an
exceptionally beautiful young woman but
one whose character and loveliness of dis- !
position make her an ideal type of Ameri
can womanhood. *' _ _

evening at the Hotel Ritz in honor of the
mother of Prince Michel Cantacuzene
of the Russian Imperial Guard, at which
formal announcement was made of the
betrothal of the Prince to Miss Julia Dent
Grant, daughter of Brigadier General
Frederick D. Grant, O. S. V.
The company Included General Horace
Porter the United States Embassador,
and Mrs. Porter; Ferdinand W. Peck, j

the Paris Exposition of UOO, and Mrs. ;

Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA CLARA, May 4.â This has been
a busy day for the Foresters of America,

A resolution was offered amending the
constitution to provide for the election of
grand officers by secret ballot. After a
protracted discussion the resolution was
defeated. A resolution was Introduced to
set apart $23 of the Grand Lodge fund
for the purpose of putting State organ
izers in the Held. This was referred to a
committee and the matter will come up
to-morrow, other matters of Importance
remain to be considered, and the Installa
tion of grand officers will complete the
work.
A grand ball at the Rose Carnival Pa
vilion on the Alameda will conclude the
week's festivities.

ney Hall and three hundred sat down to

lowing toasts were responded to: "Our

BRIGGS' ORDINATION
WILL CAUSE TROUBLE
NEW YORK. May Clerical partisans
of Dr. Briggs in the controversy that has
arisen over his proposed elevation to the
Episcopal priesthood are coming forward
with offers of their churches for his ordi
nation. It was learned to-day that St.
Bartholomew's Church and St. Andrew's
Church have been offered to Bishop Pot
ter for the ordination service.
There is and has been since the be
ginning of the controversy a disposition
among the clergy to defer radical action,
pending a decisive movement by Bishop
Potter. As one clergyman said to-day
"The Bishop has this matter in his hands
and all we can do is wait until we hear
from him."
In spite of this, however, there is no
diminution in the intensity of the oppo
sition to Dr. Briggs' ordination: Prepar
ations for hostilities are being actively
pushed and the announcement .of the day
and place of ordination will be the signal
for an open declaration of war.
Eminent theologians nave been en
gaged for a week In an examination of
Dr. Briggs' famous work, and when the
occasion arises they will be prepared to
present properly formulated charges.
WANT BRIBERS' NAMES.
COLUMBUS. Ohio, May Virgil P.
Kline and M. F. Elliott, as attorneys for
the Standard Oil Company, filed in the
Supreme Court this evening a motion to
compel Attorney General Monnett to
make more specific the statement re
cently filed by him alleging that he had
been approached by persons offering
bribes if he would dismiss the suits
against the Standard Oil Company. The
motion asks the court to require the
names to be given of all persons who are
alleged to have been connected with the
attempted bribery, not only of -Mr. Mon
nett, but also of former Attorney Gen
eral I). K. Watson.

TIDE TURNS IN
DREYFUS' FAVOR
Dupuy Canvasses Public
Sentiment in France.
Sl>eclal Dlepatcta to The Call.
NEW YORK, May 4.â dispatch to
the Commercial Advertiser from Lon-
don says: Following Constans' course
in the Boulanger episode, the Minister
of the Interior, M. Dupuy, has just ob
tained confidential . reports from pre
fects throughout France regarding the
condition of public opinion on the
Dreyfus evidence and the possible
course of the Court of Cassation and
the Government.
The weight of the reports is that ex
treme partisans on both sides remain
unmoved, but that the latest portions
of the evidence have much Impressed
the large and influential body of opin
ion which previously inclined toward
the staff and expected it would prove
its case against Dreyfus. Men of this
thinking have, from the first, been
open-minded and honest. As Lieuten
ant Colonel Henry's suicide persuaded
them to reopen the Dreyfus case, so
later evidence inclines them to a re
trial before a court-martial. The same
body of opinion is still more convinced
of the need of a- thorough reform of the
i general staff and the retirement, if not
punishment, of officers that blundered
and intruded in the Dreyfus affair.
The temper which these' reports Indi
cate prevails also in the Chambers,
though the moderates are less strong
there than outside. M. Dupuy is shap
ing his course accordingly, and there is
excellent reason to believe that two or
three weeks hence M. Ballot de Beaure
paire's report from the Court of Cassa
tion will recommend revision and re
trial, that the court, perhaps by a nar
row majority, will approve it, and that
all this will be done with the approval
and co-operation of the Government.
M. de Freycinet, furthermore, is pre
paring a plan of reorganization of the
i general, staff, particularly of the
: intelligence department. Influential
members of the military party, espe
; cially generals not smirched by Drey
fus intrigues, are meantime urging the
Government, for the sake of the whole
army, to punish the culprits as lightly
; and quietly as possible, and M. Dupuy,
I with characteristic opportunism, is
nursing a scheme by which such mild
action, with the rehabilitation of both
Dreyfus and Picquart, may square both
! sides.
GRAND ARMY VETERANS
HAVE A NARROW ESCAPE
Excursion Train Near San Diego
Saved From Destruction by a
Milkman.
SAN DIEGO, May 4.-Although the G.
A. R. encampment proper closed yester
day afternoon, many veterans are still
in the city. Two hundred of them went
by special train to-day . to Tia Juana.
There they were welcomed by the Mexi
can Judge Zaxueta, who addressed them
in English. On the return trip the G. A
R. special on the National City and Otay
i Railway had a narrow escape. A mile
, south of Sweetwater Junction an empty
! passenger train went through an open
switch. Nobody was hurt, but the train
! was partly wrecked. The G. A. R. special
was- only a- few minutes behind and a
i sharp curve shut out a view of the wreck.
I A milkman warned the engineer of the
special, which was coming at a rapid rate
and the train was stopped.
This evening several hundred of the
! veterans and other visitors were enter
| tamed at a banquet at the Second-street
! armory. Speeches were made by Past
I Commander Cahen. Commander Dill Gen
eral Salomon. Judge M. A. Luce' and
j others.

FEDERAL TROOPS
ARREST MINERS
The Men Flee in Every
Direction.
â â â¦ â
NO FAVORS SHOWN TO ANY
â â¦ â -
ALL THE STRIKERS IN BURKE
ARE TAKEN.
â¦
Strict Censorship of Telegrams -.'re
vents Disclosure of the Plans
of the General in
Command.
-
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WALLACE, Idaho, May i.â Troops
A and C, Fourth Cavalry, dismounted,
from Walla Walla and Boise, arrived
this evening. After a short halt here
both cavalry troops went direct to
Burke, arrests commencing immediate
ly. A squad was detailed as guard,
the balance of the men starting out.
As fast as men were found they were
brought to the guard and herded in the
streets. No information is given out,
but the expectations there are that prac
tically every man will be taken either
as a prisoner or a witness, his status
to be determined later. Many men
were taken in their beds. The day
shift at the Tiger mine were all taken
as they came from work. A special
train with troops left Burke at 7:30
p. m. with nearly every man in town,
twenty soldiers being left as guards.
The Mammoth, St "ird and Frisco
mines and the town of Gem will be
cleaned up while coming down the can
yon. The train left Burke with two
coaches and six box cars all loaded.
Mullan reports no arrests there to-day.
It is expected that arrests will com
mence there to-morrow. The moun
tains are full of fleein- men. With the
railroads all guarded the only safe way
out of the country is over the old
(Hidden road to Thompson Falls, Mont.
That route is lined with them. Some
take the road direct from Burke, while
others go by the way " Murray. The
road from there was ' _pt open nearly
all winter in hauling ore and is fairly
passable." Around Murray the fugi
tives also have the sympathy of a ma
jority of the people. This morning a
teamster met fifteen fugitives on that
road within six miles of here. This
afternoon the same man met fifteen
others. They acknowledged being Last
Chance men. but were frightened by
the report they had receiver! of troops
at Thompson Falls, and decided to
abandon the trip and are camping to
night at Monarch, three miles out.
They expressed themselves as feeling
had" that "hard working men should
be driven out of the country by sol
diers."
MARTIAL LAW IS
IN FULL FORCE
General Merriam in Person TaH-s
Command of Troops at
Wardner.
WARDNER, Idaho, May 4.â Four
hundred United States troops are on
guard to-night in the Coeur d'Alene
mining district and martial law is in
full force in Shoshone County, idaho.
While the Governor's proclamation, is
sued yesterday, has not been posted in
a public place citizens are aware that
martial law is. in force by reason of
the fact that United States troops are
on the ground to see that it is carried
out.
Brigadier General H. C. Merriam, who
arrived to-day from Denver by way of
Boise, has determined, if possible, to
arrest all those implicated in blowing
up the Bunker Hill and Sullivan mills.
While it is known that several of the
mob, fearing arrest, have escaped from
Shoshone County, a large majority of
those wanted are still within the
county.
General Merriam has been busy all
day sending dispatches throughout the
mining district, disposing of the troops
so as to protect property and shut off
the escape of those suspected of assist
ing in the lawless acts here last Sat
urday. Every avenue of escape from
the Cover d'Alene district is guarded
by one or more companies of troops
and the Federal authorities believe that
the arrest of all suspects will be made
without serious trouble as soon as
identification is possible.
One hundred and twenty-eight men
accused of participating in Saturday's
riot have been arrested and are under
guard of United States troops near the
railroad station here. General Merriam
has established a strict censorship over
the telegraph office here and nothing
is allowed to go out regarding the plans
for the arrest of the remaining sus
pects.
The taking of testimony in the Coro
ner's inquest over the bodies of Smith
and Cheyne, who were shot and killed
by the mob Saturday, was commenced
to-day by Coroner France behind closed
doors. Attorney General Hays of Idaho
arrived to-day and was present at the
inquest on behalf of the State. Im
portant witnesses on the stand to-day
were Sheriff James Young of Shoshone
County, JamessÃ. Sovereign, ex-master
workman of the Knights of Labor, and
Albert Hutton, a Northern Pacific en
gineer. The Attorney General refused
to disclose the nature of the testimony
until all the witnesses have been ex
amined. ' .'.
The troops arriving here to-day from
the West are sixty men from Company
B, Twenty-fourth United States Infan
try (colored), from Vancouver Barracks,
Washington, under command of Lieu
tenant Murphy, and Troop F. Fourth
Cavalry, from Boise, under Lieutenant
Walsh. Six companies of the Twenty
fourth Infantry arrived last night and
to-day from the East and will probably
be held at Burke. Wallace and Mullen.
REPUDIATED ITS CONTRACTS. |
WOODLAND, May 4.â A year ago sev
eral raisin growers In this vicinity made
a contract with a San Francisco house
for the delivery of their raisin crops at a
stipulated price. Before the season was
fairly open the price began to fall and
the firm repudiated its contracts, and as
a result the raisin growers sustained
heavy losses. A lawyer has been making
an examination of these contracts, and
declares he can collect damages. A ma
jority of the raisin growers have about
concluded to bring suit for damages or
for the fulfillment of the contract.
Big Shipment of Sheep.
WOODLAND, May 4.â One of the larg
est shipments of sheep from Woodland for
many years was made on Tuesday after
noon by Miller & Lux. The drove was
brought to this city from a range in
Capay Valley, and consisted of 3500 head.
A train of thirteen double-decked cars,
which is equal to twenty-eight ordinary
boxcars, .was required to move them. The
sheep were forwarded to the ranch of
, Miller & Lux at Madera. ,

FIERCE FIGHT FOR
SPOILS OF VICTORY
Burns Chiefly Concerned in. Get
ting Commissioned Senator.
Major McLaughlin Will Resign the Chair
manship of the Republican State
Committeeâ Gage Goes to
Sacramento.
ALL the politicians and place
seekers had something to talk
about yesterday when they read
The Call's exclusive account of
the secret conference at the Pal
ace Wednesday afternoon, in which
Governor Gage, Wendell Easton, D.
M. Burns and Major Frank McLaugh
lin discussed for three hours the sub
ject of official patronage and its distri
bution.
When the fact was made known that
the merits of various candidates had
been canvassed, and that some of the
(â¢fflce-seekers who had been accounted I
as foremost in the race ere found un- j
acceptable, diligent inquiry was made
to ascertain additional facts relating i
to the com.: ence.
Yesterday morning a cruel hoax was
perpetrated on many candidates for the
lav. of the Governor. A paragraph
was inserted in a daily paper to the
effect that Governor Gage would receive
applicants for office at his room, 714
Palace Hotel, from 10 to '1 o'clock
Thursday. Many applicants who car
ried the Republican banner last fall,
and who had grown weary of long wait
ing in the vain hop of seeing the Gov
ernor to say a word to him, went to
the room indicated and found that ii
was the apartment of Stephen M.
White. The hoax afforded cheap amuse
ment for the (.-nlookers, but the victims
were very indignant. Mr. White was
surprised to receive so many callers,
but perceiving the situation promptly
dirtcted the visitors to the rooms of the
Republican State Cenerah Committee.
It was said later in the day that the
announcement to applicants repre
sented Governor Gage's idea "i" a joke'
at the expense cf Steve White.
Politicians of high and low degree,
were in the throngs which loitered
about the court of the Palace Hotel yes
terday. It was expected that another
conference would take place, but noth
ing of the kind occurred. Governor
Gage found it difficult to get a minute
for reflection. The pressure to see him
was so keen that one man could hardly
get in a word before another bruKe In
to advocate his own or some other one's
cause.
D. .M. Burns had an extended inter
view with the Governor at a table
in the grillroom at the close of a
lunch in which the executive was
joined by Wendell ston and J. K.
Wilson.
The story goes that Burns is so deeply
concerned in his own campaign for the
Senator_.hip that he Is not vigorously
pushing the claim of any aspirants fc-r
a State .office. He has been advised by
men whose friendship he cannot ques
tion that he should demand the issue
of his comniissioi* to be United States
Senator for California. He was told
by a plain-spoken adviser to go to the
Governor and speak out in this style:
"Let us understand each other, Gov
ernor. If you intend to appoint me
United States Senator issue my com
mission now, so I can go to Matt Quay
of Pennsylvania and show him that we
are fighting for a common object and
should make the contest together."
Whether Burns used this language
or not is immaterial. It is known that
he pleaded his own cause with a deal
of earnestness. Burns does not like
to be twitted with the suggestion that
a "gold brick" has been passed to him
by the Governor. He wants a commis
sion and wants it right away.
Men high in the councils o*f the party,
who have had in some measure the con
fidence of the Governor during the past
few days, would not be surprised if
Gage issued the commission, in order
to ..able. Burns to get in with Quay
and make a light for admission to the
United States Senate.
The current of the conference day be
fore yesterday ran hard against the
friends of Major Frank McLaughlin.
He desires that two of his friends shall
be appointed to office. His heart is set
on having one of the two selected. It
is understood that Gage will appoint
one of the men so warmly indorsed, but
it is not the one on which the major's
heart is set. An explosion may be ex
pected when the major is tojd that the
appointment he 'so much desires can
not be made. The office in question is
not that of Registrar of Voters.
There is a sharp fight for the office
of Registrar. The applicants are
Colonel George Pippey, Assemblyman
Rickard and Jacob Steppacher. It Is
the talk that Gage intends to do some
thing for Steppacher, but cannot ap
point him Registrar.
There is a strong belief that the ad
jutant generalship has been settled, and
that the Governor will soon name as his
chief of staff Colonel J. C. Currier of
San Francisco. A member of the ex
ecutive committee of the Republican
State Central Committee, who is known
to be ln sympathy with Colonel Cur
rier's aspirations, received a strong in
timation from the Governor yesterday
that the colonel was slated.
In connection with the Paris Expo
sition Commission there is a story of
a broken slate. The beautiful slate on
which was penciled the name of Mark
L. McDonald of Santa Rosa has been
smashed. The original slate provided
that there should be a Republican from
Los Angeles' to represent Southern Cal
ifornia, a Democrat from the north to
represent Northern California, and. a
Republican from San Francisco to rep
resent the central region of the State.
Exposition experience or training is one
of the qualifications which the law re
quires that % each commissioner shall
possess. The Legislature appropriated
$120,000 for the show. Each commis
sioner is to receive $5000 and traveling
expenses. The original slate embraced
B. C. Truman, Republican, Los Ange
les; M. H. di Young, Republican, San
Francisco, and Mark 1.. McDonald,
Demo .rat, Santa Rosa. De Young de
clined the appointment and the report
was circulated the Governor offered
the job to W. H. Mills of the Southern
Pacific. It is an open question whether
the appointment was tendered to Mills,
but it seems to be settled that he de
clined it. - The Governor finding it no
easy, matter to get the right kind of a
Republican in San Francisco now re
solves to appoint a good Democrat to
represent. Central California. The name
of the Democrat is not divulged, but
he is a resident of San Francisco.
Taking a Democrat from this, city
means that a Republican commissioner
shall be* chosen to represent Northern
California and 'therefore Mr. McDonald
is excluded. There is still hope for the
Santa Rosa candidate, as the Governor
'is at liberty to change his mind. A re
port was circulated yesterday that the
Northern California Republican would
be M. M. Estee of Napa.
Walking Delegate Saunders, backed
by George Washington Baker, put In a
strong claim yesterday for Immediate
appointment to the office of Labor
Commissioner. I!" detached the Gov
ernor from Judge Carpenter and held
I him for ten minutes. The feat was j
performed in the presence of W. W. !
Foote, George A. Knight, ex-Secretary
of State Brown, Jesse D. Carr of Sa

Another nation has been drawn Into the

ish and are now denouncing the law of
February 20.
The Italian ship Caterina Accame is
loaded and ready to sail for Europe. Cap
tain Bellando tried hard to get a crew
for the one month's advance allowed by
the new law. but the boarding-masters
laughed at him. In consequence a crew

STABBED IN
THE DOORWAY
OF A SALOON
John Johnson Mur
derously Assaulted.
â â¢ â
HIS RECOVERY IS DOUBTFUL
â â¦ â
THE ASSAILANT A SLAVONIAN
OF BAD REPUTE.
â¦
Knockout Drops Were First Used, but
the Dose Was Miscalculated.
Robbery Is the Supposed
Motive.
John Johnson, a sailor, was stabbed by
a Slavonian, who refuses to give his
name, in the doorway of a saloon at
Jackson and Sansome streets at about 10
o'clock last night. The doctors have little
hope for his recovery.
The two men entered the saloon at
about 9 o'clock. Johnson accented the
Slavonian's Invitation to have a drink and
called for a glass of beer. While the two
men were drinking the bartender noticed
the Slavonian tampering with his com
panion's liquor, but did not pay much at
tention to him. After finishing the beer
the two started out to go to a theater.
As they were Hearing the door the sailor
suddenly put his hand to his head and
said that he could not go any farther, as
he was so dizzy he could not see.
He was helped into a chair and relaxed
Into a semi-stupor. In about half an hour
he roused up and started out with the
Slavonian. The door had hardly closed
upon them when Johnson uttered a pierc
ing shriek, followed by a fainter ejacula
tion: "Oh, I'm cutâ l'm cut bad!"
The cries attracted a crowd from inside
1 the saloon and Officers Greenwald and
. Kerrison, who were patrolling their beats
. in the vicinity,
Johnson lay on the doorstep weltering
\ in his blood, while the Slavonian, realiz
â¢ ing what he had done, started up the
street as fast as he could run. He was
pursued by the two policemen and a
crowd of citizens. lie ran directly into
his room on Montgomery near Broadway,
where he was located a few minutes later.
In one corner of the room was a basin of
I bloody water, which told its own tale of
1 how ho had attempted to conceal his guilt
by washing away the stains of his vic
tim's blood and then play innocent when
i the officers arrived. There was a great
blotch of blood on his coat sleeve near the
I wrist and another on the lapel of his coat.
I The knife with which the cutting was
done could not be found.
The would be assassin offered no resist
j ance when told he was under arrest, but
at the California-street police station he
j refused positively to give his name, and
sat sullenly In one corner of the cell.
' Johnson was put in the patrol wagon and
, hurried to the Harbor Receiving Hospital,
where it was- found that the murderous
i knife had entered his abdomen five times.
I The wounds were dressed, but the doctors
I have little hope of his recovery.
Later In the evening the Slavonian was
I sent by Sergeant Helms to the hospital,
and Johnson at once identified him as his
assailant.
Although the man refuses to divulge
his name, he is well known to the polite
as one of tit* worst characters that ever
infested the low saloons along the water
front or the deadfalls of the Barbary
Coast. He has been under arrest several
times for using knockout drops and other
offenses. He is a powerfully built man
with a blonde mustache and an evil
countenance. When Sergeant Helms or
dered two officers to remove him from the
cell that he might get a good look at him
the man fought like a demon, and would
have overpowered the men had not the
sergeant come to his assistance. In his
room on Montgomery street three bottles
were found which are supposed to con
tain knockout drops.
It is thought that his plan was to drug
Johnson In such a manner that he would
fall senseless on the street, and Mien re
lieve him of his valuables, but tne plans
miscarried. He then tried to disable him
in the doorway, but the murderous blow
produced neither death nor insensibility.
At an early hour this morning the knife
wiolder gave his name as Pomenico Ru
maz, and his nativity as Austrian.

SLOT MACHINES
DECIDED TO BE
BANKING GAMES
Twelve Saloon-Keep
ers Plead Guilty.
â¦ â -
FINED WITH NO ALTERNATIVE
â â¦-â
OTHERS DEMAND A TRIAL BY A
JURY OF THEIR PEERS. .&
Judge Groezinger Fails to See Ary
Difference Between Check
Machines and Card
Machines.
The crusade against nickel-in-the-slot
machines last December has not met with
the success which was anticipated. The
cases were originally assigned to Judge
Treadwell's court and. he convicted sev
eral of the defendants, but on the cases
being appealed the judgment was re
versed, as. the arrests had been made un
der the section of the Penal Code which
refers to lotteries, instead of section 330,
which refers to banking games. Then
arrests were made under section 330.
yesterday twelve of the defendants
pleaded quilting. in Acting Police Judge
Groezinger's court and agreed not to use
any of the machines in their saloons
again. Their names were Edward Lun
stedt, Richard Carr, William Spahr, Al
Whit.'. Alfred Dohlman, J. C. Myles, Wil
liam Bogen, Alfred Bradsay, William Pe
ters. A. Bergstrom. M. J. Ward and M.
J. Boyce. The machines they used were
those that passed out checks redeemable
at the tear by money or drink.*.
The Judge said he had no reason to
doubt that the defendants would keep
their promise and he thought the ends of
justice would be subserved by their keep
in",* the machines out of their saloons.
lie would therefore Impose a nominal
fine.
Prosecuting Attorney Carpenter re
minded the Judge that under section 330
the lowest penalty was a fine of $100.
".Well," said the Judge, "1 will make
the fine In each case $100 with no alterna
tive, but if they should again be charged
with a similar offense the fine would be
$100 with the usual alternative. I see no
difference between these machines and
the machines used in cigar stores. The
one is as much a banking game as the
other, and why the police can see a dis
tinction is beyond my comprehension.
Judge Morrow decided in the Federal
court in a case regarding an Infringe
ment of a copyright on these cigar ma
chines that they were purely banking
games and had no right to get a pat
ent."
Fourteen other cases were continued till
May 17, as the defendants pleaded not
guilty and will ask for a trial by jury.
There are. including these, altogether
about thirty cases in the different courts
yet to be tried.
Chief Lees did not like the decision of
Acting Judge Groezinger in making "no
alternative." He said that the only way
to do was for the Board of Supervisors
to instruct the City and County Attorney
to institute civil suits against each of the
defendants for the recovery of the fine.
The matter was out of his hands. He
had no hesitation in saying that these
machines were rank robberies and filched
the hard earned money out of the pockets
of the workingman. Regarding machines
in cigar stores he had only to say that
the Board of Supervisors licensed them.
Trouble Over a Courtroom.
Judge Graham and Acting Judge (.roe- !
singer had a dispute yesterday morning
as to the temporary occupancy of Judge
Conlan's courtroom. Judge Groezlnger's
bailiff had his prisoners in the dock first,
and Judge Graham's bailiff had to make
a masterly retreat. Judge Graham de
manded possession of the courtroom and
Judge Conlan was called In to decide. He
decided in favor of Groezinger, as Groe
zinger had taken over the cases on his
calendar. Graham, who is presiding
Judge, has called a meeting for this
morning of the three Judges and is deter
mined to have the courtroom.

FORGES A BROTHER'S
NAME AND DECAMPS
Howard Emerson Is a Victim of
Race Track Gambling.
Well-Known Centerville Attorney Disap
pears and His Affairs Are Said to
Be in a Very Bad Muddle.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, May 4.
HOWARD EMERSON, a well-known
lawyer and politician of Center
ville. is missing, and racetrack
gambling has scored another vic
tim. Emerson disappeared last Tuesday
and now all kinds of charges against him,
including misappropriation of funds in
trusted to his care, forgery and embez
zlement, are in circulationâ the race
track is held responsible for it all, as the
man who is looked upon as an absconder,
even by the members of his own family,
is said to have been an Inveterate gam
bler, his favorite field of operations being
the Emeryville resort. Almost the last
act of Emerson before his disappearance
was to forge the name of his brother,
Dr. H. W. Emerson, health officer at Cen
terville, to a number of checks, which he
cashed. He also hypothecated securities
belonging to his sister, and it is strongly
suspected that he has managed to obtain
other sums by equally illegal methods.
He was the attorney for several large
estates at Centerville. among them be
ing the James Graham, C. J. Stevens and
Fair estates, and his affairs are said to
be so badly mixed that It will be Impossi
ble to determine the extent of his mis
deeds without a thorough examination of
his accounts by an expert.
Emerson came to Oakland last Tues
day, the day of his disappearance, and
requested J. B. Lanktree, a money
broker, to cash seven checks for $60 each,
signed by his brother, the physician. He
was given the money, and from that time
nothing has been seen of him. He has
dropped out of sight as completely as
though the earth had opened and swal
lowed him.
To-day Dr. Emerson, alarmed by his
brother's continued absence, came to
Oakland to make inquiries as to his
whereabouts. He failed to locate the
LAKEVIEW ROAD.
Mrs. Dr. Merritt's Demand Bars the
Way to the Extension of
the Line.
The Sunnyslde Improvement Club held
an Important meeting last night. The at
titude of Dr. Emma Sutro Merritt on
the proposed Lakeview railroad was dis
cussed at length, and near the close of j
the meeting it was decided to send a del- j
egation to A. B. Spreckels, the president
of the San Francisco and San Mateo
Electric Railroad, with a view to bring
ing about an amicable settlement.
During the â¢ discussion it was shown
that the owners of -the street railway
were anxious to extend. the line on Sun- i
nyslde .avenue to the -'lngleside coursing
grounds and on Plymouth avenue, via the
Lakeview district, to the ocean road.
â¢.The only obstacle to the purposed, im- j
provement comes from Mrs. Merritt. who I
wants the company to pay the cost of
grading, sewering, macadamizing and ;
sidewalking along the entire right of way.
Her demand was refused by the railroad
company, which stated Its willingness to
pave its portion of the roadway. In mak- I
ing its declination the railroad officials I
stated that they were unwilling to put
Mrs. Merritt's entire property In condition
for the privilege of securing a right of
way through her land. As such an exten
sion would be of material benefit to the
residents of the Sunnyside district an ef- !
fort will be made to bring about a com- ,
promise.
The double track now under construe- I
tion from Thirtieth street to the ceme
teries by the San Francisco and San Ma
teo Railroad Company has been looked j
1 upon as a forerunner of prosperity by the ;
' people living along the line of road, as it
will result in their securing speedier
' transit and easier access to the downtown
; portions of the city.
THE NEW CURATOR.
Reception at the Mark Hopkins In-
stitute of Art This Evening.
This evening at the Mark Hopkins In
stitute of Art there will be a reception
to Captain Robert Howe Fletcher, the
new curator of the Art Association. All
of the members of the association and
the teachers and students of the school
have been invited.
As an army officer, a writer of stories
and a contributor of high jinks papers
Captain Fletcher is a success. What his
record will be as curator, and especially
a curator at his own reception, remain-*
to be determined. Observation will be
taken of his bearing this evening. If he
shows the least sign of agitation Joseph
D. Grant and Horace Piatt will be handy
to reinforce him. As a platform talker
Captain Fletcher has merit. On ordinary
occasions he scorns to produce manu
script to refresh his memory or establish
any other signal of mental distress. It is
expected that eloquent music and charm
ing company will welcome him to his new
station of duty.

MECHANICS AND CONTRACTORS.
Harmonious Condition of Affairs Ex-
isting Between the Two Bodies.
There was a spirit of go, feeling mani
fested at the meeting of the delegates to
the Builders' and Trades Council last
night. It was announced that the
contractors had cheerfully agreed to the
recent rules adopted by the Carpenters'
Union. To show the appreciation of the
action the Builders' Trades Union passed
a vote of thanks to the contractors.
Grand Organizer R. L. Malsbarv re
ported that Painters' Union No. 73 was or
ganized and in. good shape to make its
influence felt ... among the other -labor
unions. This union will be composed of
all painters and decorators In this city
and none but first class workmen will be
admitted. A membership in the union will
be a certificate to those employing paint
ers and decorators that they are getting
flrst classsmen to do their work. Dele
gates James Massey. E. H. Windsor and
Thomas. Linton were admitted as mem
bers of the Builders' and Trades Council.
A report on the condition of labor in
San Francisco was received from Organ
izer Malsbary. In which Be stated that
everything looked bright and cheerful for
the wage-earners, since the utmost har
mony existed between the employers and
the employes.
The council passed a resolution com
mending the daily press and the police for
the stand taken against the gambling sa
loons along the line of Market street.
Attorney C. G. Nagle appeared before

A SMUGGLER CAUGHT.
Customs Inspector Harrigan caught a
smuggler very cleverly yesterday morn
ing at 4 o'clock, and the smuggler, whose
name is William Clay, is at present re
posing in the County Jail. At the hour
named Harrigan saw Clay stealing along
the wharf to a pile of lumber, behind
which he disappeared. Harrigan followed
him and saw him receive a small tele
scope basket which some unknown per
son had let down from one of the dead
lights of the forecastle of the steamer
Curacao. Harrigan grabbed Clay and the
basket and found the plunder to be 475
Mexican cigars, valued at from 10 to 12
cents each.
Upon the fact of the seizure being re
ported to Deputy Surveyor St. John that
official ordered all hands to make a
thorough search of the steamer, which
was done, and 525 more smuggled cigars
were found by Inspector Lundqulst in
the forepeak. concealed among a pile of
life preservers.
Raided a Lottery i tace.
Sergeant Shea and posse yesterday raid
ed the rooms on Kearny street and seized
a number of lottery tickets of the Original
Little Honduras and Original Little Bene
ficencia companies. Henry S. Kahn,
the manager of the companies, was ar
rested and booked at the City Prison on
a charge of keeping a lottery.

SEAL HERD SCATTERED.
| But Little Left of the Point Reyes
Rookeries.
It is very evident that the sea lion herd
of Point Reyes is pretty well broken up
and scattered by the hunters who have
been for the past few days gunning for
the big marine beasts. The onslaught
proper was made on Monday, the hunters
emptying their magazine rifles Into the
rookeries where the herd was taking a
sun bath. After that Deputy Wilson con
tinued the killing, picking off the seals
one by one as they went by in the en
deavor to get back to the ledges on which
they were wont to bask. Yesterday a let
ter was received from Mr. Wilson, who
wrote from Point Reyes under the date
of May 2, as follows:
The lions that are here now are most all of
the big gray variety and cannot be -worked
from my ..id points. They are more 'â¢: th..
deep-water kind, bo to do anything with them
I had to get into the rookery, which was no
easy task. I out some steps into the rock to
day, so I think I can get into their camp to
morrow on a rope. I will shoot on the out
posts until they leave camp, then take mv po
sition and wait their return. I do not think
there will be enough here to hold me but a
few days. For some reason there has not been
many here since my last visit.
One Who has not been on the ground
has no idea of the difficulty and dangers
attending the project of Mr. Wilson to
get down to the rookeries proper. The
"roosting" places of the seals are located
at the foot of cliffs that rise from the
water to a height of 700 feet. The favorite
resorts are in a circular cove" protected
on the north, east and south by perpen
dicular bluffs, down which it is impossi
ble to descend unless the adventurer does
as Mr. Wilson has .doneâ cut steps. into
the solid rocks. Even then it is likely
that it will lie necessary to use a rope to
get to the water near the rookeries. By
the time Mr. Wilson empties a few hun
dred more cartridges into the stragglers
the herd will be so badly broken and de
moralized that it will he a long time be
fore they collect there again in any num
bers.

was extinguished in a few minutes.
