PURE FOOD LAW
IS BADLY NEEDED
To Protect Buyers in
America.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CHICAGO. May i.â The Senatorial Pure
Food investigating Committee held its
second session here to-day. Giles Lewis,
a druggist of this city, testified that while
i other countries prevented the selling of
; adulterated goods "within their own bor
ders no provisions were made against ex
porting them to foreign countries. He de
clared that the United States received
articles made In Germany and France that
; could not be sold in the country "where
i they were made because of the preserva
: lives used. Mr. Lewis said adulteration
I could be divided into two classes, one of
j which was the adding of coloring matter
' for the sake of appearance and the other
was to give fictitious values to articles of
I food. His advice to the committee was
j that a national pure food law be installed
and a permanent commission be installed
in office, the duty of which would be to
prevent the marketing of any article of
food or drink, domestic or imported,' that
was misrepresented. The limit of adul
teration, he declared, should be governed
by the commission, and the committee
should analyze all articles and certify to
their purity by a Government stamp.
Dr. Wiley next took the stand anu gave
information to the committee regarding
the articles sold in this country which
were adulterated. Among practices that
are fraudulent, yet not injurious to the
health, he mentioned the sale of fish* of
cheap variety and grade for more ex
pensive kinds, especially fish packed in
oil. It also is a common custom. Dr.
Wiley said, for dealers to stamp pack
ages with foreign stamps and deceive the
public into believing it was buying im
ported goods. Horse flesh, according to
the expert, was commonly used for food
purposes in European countries and la
beled as beef, but he did not know of its
| being used to any great extent in this
' country. The sales of turtles in restau
| rants for terrapin was also a common
means of defrauding the public.
Famous wines and other drinks of for
eign countries are on sale on American
markets that never saw the color of the
ocean.
Adulterated food and drink which are
injurious to the public health, the witness
said, could be classed in two groupsâcol
oring and preserving. The coloring mat
ter was used principally because it was
pleasing to the eye and made the food
palatable.
The matter which is used principally
in restoring the color of green peas, the
witness declared, sometimes was salt of
zinc and sometimes salt of copper, either
of which is poisonous, and the use of
which should not be permitted. "Probably
the chemical most used as a preservative,
according to Dr. Wiley, Is salycilic acid,
which is made to a great extent from car
bolic acid. It should not be permitted, he
said, because It was very injurious, espe
cially to those who have weak stomachs.
He ended his testimony by Baying that no
food offered for sale which contained pre
servatives was tit to eat.
THE MURDOCK CASE
NEARING THE END
Witness Powers Loses Some of His
Positiveness Under Severe
Cross-Examination.
WILLOWS, May 4.â the opening of
court this morning, in the Murdock suit.
Jack Powers was recalled for further
cross-examination. He seemed to lose
some of his positiveness that was prom
inent yesterday. Adam and Ira Comp
ton testified to a trip Ira Compton and
William Murdock made to Modoc Coun
ty in 1887. This testimony was directed
to showing that William Murdock was
not at the State Fair in 1887. Not much
success was made, however, in that line.
The defense then Introduced a lot of cop
ies of the records of Tehama County,
which they claimed went to contradict
Witness Ashurst and G. W. Murdock.
The whole financial condition of Tehama
was gone into and put in evidence, but
showed little more than that Tehama
County was in a fair condition in 1877-78.
G. G. Murdock and his sister. * Lizzie
Smith, swore to a conversation they had
with the plaintiff in 1881 and 1887, and sev
eral, letters of a social nature were* read
in evidence to show that the relation of
the Lake County Murdochs were friendly
with' the plaintiff and her husband in
1881-87. Â:.'
Mary Compton, sister of William Mur
dock, wished to testify as to what Mur-i
dock said during his last illness concern
ing the note in suit, but was shut out by
the court. Several depositions were read,
the main one being that of plaintiff,
taken last year.
Just before adjournment the defense
offered a lot of depositions looking to the
impeachment of Witness Ashurst, but
plaintiff's attorneys . objected and this
matter will be argued to-morrow morn
ing. The end seems near. â¢:â¢"
Death of a Bedding Man.
REDDING, May James Marshall
Bryan died in this city at 5 o'clock this
morning of typhoid malaria. He was the
senior member of the firm of Bryan Bros.,
up to May 1 the lessees of the Golden
Eagle Hotel in this city. He leaves a
Widow and four small children and a
brother and a sister residing in Oakland.
He was a native of Sierra County, Cali
fornia, aged 36 years.. The remains will
be shipped to Oakland Friday night. The
mother and father of the deceased are
buried there. The funeral will be under
the auspices of the Knights of Pythias,
of which order the deceased was a promi
nent member. â '".-, - ;
Cannot Appear for Trial.
SUISUN, May The trial of Jesse
Walters, on a charge of having murdered
Alfred Cook in Napa last January, will
not take place next Wednesday, the date
set for the trial. Walters has been con
fined to his bed from the effects of a
shot he received in the thigh at the time
of the tragedy, and the attending physi
clan, Dr. W. G. Downing, notified Sheriff
Savage to-day that It would be impos
sible for Walters to appear. May 20 will
probably be the new date set.
WILL TIE UP
LAKE COMMERCE
Buffalo Strikers Control
Situation.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
BUFFALO, May 4.â The grain shov
elers and the freight-handlers have
pronounced their ultimatum to the
Lake Carriers'. Association. At a mass
meeting this .afternoon, it was an
nounced amid great applause that un
less the Lake Carriers' Association ac
ceded to the demands of the men by
to-morrow noon, the entire commerce
of the lakes from Buffalo to Duluth
would be tied up.
The hall was filled and a crowd stood
about on the outside. They were or
derly, but determined. The meeting
was addressed by John M. Hennessy.
â President P.. J. McMahon, of the new
shovelers' union;. M. J. Foley, repre
senting the men employed in the ele
vators, and others.
Mr. McMahon announced that the
time had come when the whole plan !
of the movement could be made
known.
"We have everything in our power."
he said. "Unless the Lake Carriers'
Association comes to our terms by to
morrow at noon, the commerce of Buf
falo and along the lakes will be tied up.
We have been working on this matter
for weeks and know what we are do
ing. If we want financial help we can
get all we need." *". :- j
He thanked the men- for the good"
judgment they had shown in keeping
quiet and orderly, and cautioned them
about getting into any trouble. In
front of the hall there were about 700
Poles, members of the new Freight
Handlers' Union, and the speeches
were translated into Polish for them.
M. J. Foley announced, amid loud
cheers, that to-morrow at noon, every
man now working in an elevator would
quit.
"All we want is" what is fair." he said,
"but we will stand by you men to the
end. The coal handlers who have been
serving the Lake Carriers' vessels have
already quit work, the engineers will
go out to-morrow and the contractors
will do no more work on the docks in
Buffalo."
FURTHER EVIDENCE
AGAINST DESIRELLO
Prosecution of the Slayer of James
Johnston at Colma Js Almost
Completed.
REDWOOD CITY, May 4.-The third
day of the trial of Fred Desirello for the
murder of James Johnston ended this
afternoon without any startling develop
ments. Since yesterday afternoon the
time has been devoted to the examination
and cross-examination of witnesses for
the prosecution. This morning the pro
ceedings began with the cross-examina
tion .of John Vaccari. The defense laid
great stress, upon the fact that at the
Coroner's inquest witness testified that
the defendant said he shot to kill, while
at the preliminary examination he ad
mitted on cross-examination that the
words used were shot to kill the horses
This coincides with the theory of the de
fense that Desirello shot at the horses to
bring them to a halt, as he supposed they
were running away. Henry Jensen, the
third boy upon the wagon at the time of
the shooting, gave testimony similar to
that of Vaccari. He corroborated Vac
cari in the statement made by defendant
"Shut your mouth, or I'll put a bullet
through you," when Vaccari asked him
why he shot at them. The cross-examina
tion of the witnesses has been conducted
by Judge E. F. Fitzpatrick and has been
very exhaustive' in an effort to shake the
testimony. Before court adjourned three
other witnesses for the prosecution gave
evidence, viz.â lda Ravn, Lillie Ravn and
John Rockford. The prosecution will
probably close some time to-morrow, and
possibly the defense will commence the
Introduction of evidence. It is likely, how
ever, that the case wil not go to the jury
before Saturday afternoon. . .
QUARTZ DISCOVERED IN
LOS ANGELES COUNTY
Valuable Ledge Tapped Near the
Surface That Assays $10 a Ton
in Gold.
FALLBROOK STATION, Cat,', May .4â
Arthur Coventry, a young Englishman,
has been prospecting his ranch near
Fallbrook. This is an old settled country
and no one dreamed that anything of a
mineral nature" would ever be developed
here, but as Coventry went on excavating
and prospecting people began to take
more interest. Yesterday he came to a
ledge in a tunnel thirty feet below the
surface. He finds the ledge ten feet wide
of a hard quartz, containing sul
phites, easily worked by the cyanide
process, and shows an assay of $10
per ton in gold and considerable silver
Upon the discovery other farmers
.began to prospect and found a large part
of the country composed of the same kind
of rock, which shows gold in paying
quantities from the surface. While the
assays are low, the quantity of the ore
th'_ ease with which it can be reduced
and the location of the district will serve
to increase its value. The prospects are
within a mile of the railroad station and
near a good stream of water, the Santa
Margatita River.

the coast.

SPAIN TO DEMAND
CHINESE TERRITORY
Collecting Evidence to
That End.
_ Â»
Special Dispatch to The Call.
. HONGKONG, May 4.â Spain is col
lecting evidence here with a view to
claiming a cession of land in China as
indemnity from the Chinese Govern
ment for permitting the steamer Abbey
to leave Canton last August with arms
for the Filipinos.
The little steamer Abbey, or Abby,
was seized by the armed dispatch boat
of Admiral Dewey's squadron on Sep
tember 23 last. She was of American
register, though formerly known as the
Pasig. It was reported to Admiral
Dewey that she was carrying arms and
supplies of war to the Filipinos and he
sent the McCuiloch to intercept her.
When the American cutter finally
found the Abbey in Batangas Bay, on
the southern" coast of Luzon, the fllli
buster had landed her cargo of arms
and ammunition and the insurgents re
fused to give them up. Thereupon the
Abbey was seized and taken by the
McCuiloch to Manila, in accordance
with Admiral Dewey's desire to main
tain the status quo in the Philippines
pending the conclusion of the work of
the Peace Commission in Paris.
YOUNGEST CRIMINAL
IS AGAIN AT LARGE
Ricardo Machado, Suspected of Mur-
der and Convicted of Burglary,
Escapes From Whittier.
LOS ANGELES, May 4.â Ricardo Ma
chado, who is probably ; the youngest
criminal that was ever placed behind the
bars of any jail in California, is again at
large. . '
Ricardo is a little Mexican who hails
from San Pedro, and during his residence
on earth of nine years has been tried on a
charge of murder and was a few weeks
ago convicted in the Superior Court of
burglary. Several months ago his play
mate, Willie Schmidt, was shot, and died
a few days late. As ne and Ricardo had
gone out together on that fatal afternoon
Ricardo was accused of the crime and
lav in jail for several weeks. He told a
plausible story while on the witness
stand, declaring that the gun simply went
off and that was all he could tell about
the affair. Between sobs he clutched his
overalls, that came to his knees, and with
his unkempt hair he presented a most
woebegone picture. When the jury re
turned a verdict of not guilty his parents
took him home, but he soon forgot his
troubles and was caught in the act of rob
bing a candy store. For this crime he
was speedily found guilty and later sent
to the Whlttler Reform School under sen
tence to remain until he became of age.
To-day the big whistle at the school
sounded the warning that Ricardo had
levanted, and the ranchers and officers
are scouring the country for the runa
way. \: :V- : 1"

PILGRIMAGE OF PYTHIANS.
Santa Cruz Preparing" to Welcome the
Grand Lodge.
SANTA CRUZ. May 4.-Avalin Lodge,
Knights of Pythias, is making elaborate
preparations for the entertainment of the
Grand Lodge, Knights of Pythias, which
will be held in this city during the week
commencing Monday, May 15. On Monday
the delegates will be met at the train by
the band and the reception committee, of
which ex-Lieutenant Governor Jeter is
chairman. In the evening at the armory
a reception will be held. There will be
addresses of " welcome, responses and
speeches and a promenade concert. Dur
ing the. evening the Grand Lodge will be
called to order.
On Tuesday the Grand Lodge will be in
session from 9 to 11 o'clock. From 11 to 2
o'clock a bullshead dinner will be given
at the Dolphin Baseball Park. In the
evening the D. O. K. K. are to make their
annual pilgrimage.
Wednesday will be devoted to Grand-
Lodge sessions. In the evening Avalin

SONOMA OFFICER DEAD.
City Marshal Robin, a Pioneer, Sue-
cumbs to Illness.
SONOMA, May 4.-City Marshal Robin
died at his home in this place last night
at 9:25 o'clock after a brief illness. He
was one of the pioneers of Sonoma. He
located in this place in 1854 and has re
sided here ever since, with the exception
of one year spent at his old home In
France in 1892. Mr. Robin was very pop
ular, and held the office of City Marshal
for consecutive terms. He was an Odd
Fellow, and was 60 years of age. He
leaves a daughter and numerous nephews
and nieces, the latter residents of San
Francisco. The funeral will take place
under the auspices of the Odd Fellows
Saturday morning.
Snow Blockade Broken.
DENVER. May 4.â The first train over
the South Park line Into Dillon for four
months ran through solid walls of snow
to-day and steamed into town amid the
blowing of whistles and the ringing of
bells. The snow blockade had been broken.
Work is now progressing toward Lead
ville. and the entire South Park line from
Denver to the big camp will soon be open,
it is said at the general superintendent's
office, early next week.

PflY GAS CHARGES, BUT PROTEST
WHILE the question of the rate the gas com
pany can legally charge consumers is in doubt,
the company is faking full advantage of the
situation and is sending out bills at the old rate of
$1 75 per thousand feet and is making every effort to
collect them! It is therefore important the ratepayers
should take notice of their rights under the circum
stances and act accordingly.
For the purpose of saving his legal rights the con
sumer should, when the bill is presented, decline to
pay at the rate of Si 75 and make the collector a
tender of payment at the rate fixed by the Super
visors of Si 10 per thousand. The collector will
doubtless refuse to accept payment at thai rate, and
then notice will be served by the company that unless
the bill is paid as rendered the meter will be taken
from the consumer's premises and the gas shut off.
Under that threat the consumer should go to the
office of the gas company, pay the amount extorted,
but at the same time make a formal protest against the
payment of a rate greater titan Si 10. The result of
such a course will be that when the courts have pas-cd
upon the question, and have upheld the rate fixed by
the Supervisors, the consumer will have a right to re
cover from the company all that has been paid by him
in excess of the amount of legal charges.
If the protest he not made no part of the sum ex
torted by the company can be recovered, as the pay
ment in such case will be held in law to have been
voluntary. If. however, the bill be paid under protest,
and only because it is extorted by a threat to remove
the meter and shut off the gas. the payment is involun
tary and the excess extorted by the company must
be refunded. The right course to be pursued, there
fore, is to pay, but protest. The gas company can
be compelled to respect the law. if the public is firm
in upholding its rights.
AN EASTERN VIEW OF IT.
COMMENTING upon the question of the right
of a Governor to appoint a United States Sen
ator when a Legislature fails to elect, the Bos
ton Herald points out that the announced intention of
the executive of California to appoint Burns, taken in
connection with the appointment of Quay, confirms
the wisdom of the framers of the constitution in de
nying such powers to the Governors of States. The
Herald says:
"These cases in Pennsylvania and in California are
strictly on a par in one important respect. The Sen
ators elected by the respective Governors are both
of them men whom the people, acting through their
Legislatures, have refused to elect. The most
strenuous man in asserting the Governor's power will
hardly claim that the constitution intended that he
should use it to veto an expressed decision of the peo
ple, yet this is what will be done if these two men
from Pennsylvania and California are admitted to
seats in the Senate. The reason and the equity of
the case, as well as the law, as again and again in
terpreted, forbid Such claimants to seats from being
acce; *
The soundness 01 that statement is not to be de
nied. The- Herald occupies the position of an impar
tial student of the situation. From the distance of
Boston it has no affiliations with factions or State
politics either in California or in Pennsylvania. It
surveys the .Senatorial contests from. the standpoint
of national politics only, and its judgment, therefore,
cannot be attributed to any bias or prejudice against
either Burns or Quay.
The point made by the Herald does not of course
affect the legal argument, for that turns upon the
power of a Governor to appoint without reference to
the character of the man appointed. It does affect
the issue, however, as a matter of fact, since it shows
the importance of upholding a strict construction of
the constitution in this respect.
It has been said often. of the framers of the con
stitution, "They builded wiser than they knew," and
the provision of the government on this point may
be taken as one of the evidences of it. â  Certainly
in the light of present experience, if we had the con
struction of*, the constitution to go over again, the
danger of permitting Governors to appoint Senators
would be guarded against as carefully as it was by
the fathers, for we # have seen what, the fathers but
guessed at, that, were such powers conferred, Gov
ernors of States would intrigue to prevent the elec
tion of truly representative men to the Senate by
Legislatures in order to obtain for themselves the
opportunity of appointing some unworthy favorite of
their own. .

ported to have said he is in England for the purpose
of having a rest, and now the country would like to
know who was'disturbir^-him, &<WAJikMwiÂ£* x ,
M. M. ESTEE'S BLUNDER.
ALL patriotic citizens in the American republic
/ \ united with the deepest enthusiasm in their
â¢â  * recognition of the honor due to -Admiral
Dewey, in his personal and in his representative
character. But Mr. Estee seized the opportunity of
his selection as one of the speakers on "Dewey day"
to deliver an unjust, virulent and t inexcusable tirade
against millions of Americans who oppose the policy
of foreign annexation or conquest. His address was
as palpable a violation of the proprieties of the occa
sion as the philippic of Senator Stewart at Oakland
in 1869 in favor of the Chinese, which desecrated our
national anniversary.
Mr. Estee is the Handy Andy of the Pacific Coast.
He can be safely relied upon to commit any possible
blunder upon any public occasion. During the last '
session of the Legislature he blatantly advocated the
candidacy of Dan Burns, and thus involuntarily
helped the public by contributing to his defeat. He
has now set up a non-existing administration policy,
and by his unsolicited contribution to the numerous
misrepresentations of the attitude of the Government
has furnished nutritious arguments against his own
opinions.
It would be tedious to follow Mr. Estee through
his labyrinthine fulmination. But a few of his sen
tences may be profitably segregated and their ab
surdity exposed. Evidently misled by the Examiner's
perversions of Thomas Jefferson, instead of deriving
his information from original sources, he directly re
verses that great statesman's expressed opinions and
presents him as an advocate of expansion in foreign
countries. He then oracularly states that ''the nation
will grow fastest which offers to the world the best
government and the greatest amount of good unoc
cupied land." This correct proposition, the most ob
vious argument for adherence to our constitutional
system, and for the development of the American re
public on this continent, is one of Mr. Estee's rea
sons for insisting upon Asiatic expansion! What rot!
It is beyond controversy that unless revolutionized by
the imperialists we have' the "best government" in
existence, and that there is abundance of "good un
occupied land" in California, in Texas, in Arizona
and in the greater part of the Union. We have land
and resources that would easily support five hundred
millions of Americans, living in harmony and it.
prosperity under their constitutional Government.
"As the Philippine archipelago is far more densely
populated than the United States, even if the climate
were not prohibitory to our farmers and our horti
culturists, there is no room for them in that unin
viting and distant group of Asiatic remnants.
Mr. Estee also follows the monotone of expansion in
attempting to analogize our unsuccessful management
cf a few scattered Indian tribes in this vast continent
to the task of subduing and governing ten millions of
Filipinos. If our own concerns are insufficient to
satisfy our energy and our cupidity, and there is a
demand that this Government should become a politi
cal missionary, extending freedom by the use of ar
tillery, it would be preferable to take up the cause of
the Armenians or the Finns and enforce our civiliza- i
tion upon the Turks and the Russians.
In another part of his address he wantonly abused ;
every American citizen who. in opposition to the
policy of Asiatic expansion, has exercised his inalien- j
able rights, guaranteed by the Declaration of Inde- j
pendence and by the constitution. He paraphrased â 
the observation of General Merritt that we have "out
grown the constitution" into the weaker expression
that we have "grown beyond our youthful restraints,", j
and characterized millions of Americans, who are
simply loyal to their institutions, as "the enemies of
our country." He also falsified the record by saying:
"We would have had no war with- the Filipinos if
dissatisfied Americans had not sought to make the
Filipinos believe that we were bent on their oppres
sion." This is a nice assertion to make and a nice
doctrine to* be proclaimed by an aspiring politician
who has frequently and unsuccessfully solicited the j
suffrages of his fellow citizens. In 1882 and again in
1806 Mr. Estee should have announced his opinion
that dissatisfaction among Americans and the free
discussion of questions affecting their constitution
and their national policy should be suppressed be
cause they tended to encourage semi-barbarism six
thousand miles away to resist invasion on their own
soil. He was twice and overwhelmingly beaten as it
was. If he had revealed his conversion to despotism
his first defeat would have been his last.
Finally Mr. Estee uses the mercenary argument as
a sort of climax to his partisan harangue on a day
sacred to American nationality: "We have bought
these islands and paid for them when we could have
got them by conquest." We purchased the abstract
pretensions of Spain to the Philippines and found
that we had secured no element of title, not even
naked possession. We are now engaged in the ap
plication of imperial 'precedents in the distinct line of
conquest. This may have been forced upon us by
the treaty' of Paris. But, if we had been contented
with the crushing victories over Spain, had returned
to our ordinary business of enlarging and improving
citizenship, had maintained the Monroe doctrine, had
adhered rigidly to our own principles and had con
secrated ourselves afresh to the fulfillment of our
legitimate mission, the Filipinos would not have been
puzzled by their relegation, in their own home, to the
ranks of "rebels" and "insurgents," and there would
have been no effort to ingraft imperial shoots on the
American vine, rooted in our revolutionary epoch. and
yielding delicious fruits to humanity.
Mr. Estee- is welcome to his witches' caldron of
contradictory sentiments. The Call prefers its
Americanism and the peaceful expansion of consti
tutional liberty.
BACKING THE PEACE CONFERENCE.
IF the coming peace, conference fail to accomplish
anything of good for the world it will not be for
lack of support among the people in any part of
the civilized world. Peace associations of all kinds
have been busily engaged in a campaign of education
on the subject ever since the Czar's invitation to the
powers was first made public, and arrangements have
been prepared lor making an international demon
stration of peace sentiment during the sessions of
the conference. . â¢
As was to have been expected, the lead in the in
ternational demonstration has been taken by the
ladies. Mrs. May Wright Sewall, the American-mem
ber of "The International Peace and Arbitration
Commission,"" has sent' out circulars announcing that
simultaneous meetings of women will be held May
15 in the more important towns all over the civilized
world. It is the programme that at the meetings the
women will proclaim their universal and unanimous
idea of peace and the promotion of the principle of
justice (instead of force) in the international relations
of peoples. Besides, they will interchange addresses
of sympathy with all the other women gathered for
the same cause and at the same hour in other coun
tries.
The support given to the conference, however, will
not be confined to that extended by the ladies of the
international demonstration and by professional peace
societies. There exists in the commercial and finah
;*ia_ world and 4n the higher circles of statecraft --a
THE SA.N FRANCISCO CALL. FRIDAY, MAY 5, 1899.
strong desire to see something of real value to the
cause of peace achieved by the movement now. under
way. London, being the center of the commerce and
finance of the time, is naturally the point at which
peace propositions are most cordially received, and
accordingly public- sentiment there is more sanguine
cf good results from the conference than that of per
haps any other large city.
The earnestness with which the British people have
taken up the cause of peace is shown by the recent
statement of the Bishop of London: "No subject has
been received with such complete enthusiasm and
unanimity as this subject of the promotion of peace;
there is no subject on which the English people' have
been approached for a long time in which they have
â¢shown such deep interest. And this is the more re
markable, for the English public are not given to the
expression of ideas in the abstract."
Mr. Balfour, leader of the House of Commons,
has made himself conspicuous also among the san
guine promoters of the cause. In a speech a short
time ago he declared his conviction that if war were
not to; be extinguished it would be at least greatly
diminished. He then went on to say: "Since the
conclusion of the great revolutionary wars this coun
try has only once been at war with a civilized power.
That is to say, in a period of more than eighty years
our peace with the civilized nations of the earth has
only been disturbed for a single period of about three
years. Compare that with what went on in the last
century. A man of 40 at the present time has never
seen war in this country."
There is no- gainsaying the strength of public sen
timent in favor .peace. All the same, the suppres
sion of Finland has been accomplished, the nations
go on increasing their armaments, and so small a
matter as a witty speech on the part of a witty cap
tain of the navy so arouses the war passions of a great
people like the Germans that they are ready to tax
themselves to build up a greater navy just to relieve
their feelings.
GOVERNMENT IN BUSINESS.
IN Los Angeles is a sort of Aaron's rod club,
called "The New Democracy," which is organized
for the purpose of swallowing all the other fac
tions, fragments, 1 crowns, scepters, creeds, platform.-,
muniments of power and evidences of authority in the
party.
In conformity to its high title, it is new, if not
fresh.
There have been new movements in the party be
fore, but they were simply in the form of changes in
its drill, tactics, manual of arms and field formation.
Such was Vallandigham's "Dayton platform," in
which, after the war, he amnestied and forgave the
Republican party and . advised taking up issues of
revenue and matters that did not relate to the Civil
War. its cause, continuance and result. But the Los
Angeles newness is newer than anything that has
heretofore been proposed. The club had announced
itself for all the new ideas in finance. It must be
confessed that these, ideas are very old, have been
often tried and abandoned as unscientific and un
tenable. But the Los Angeles club has just heard of
them, and therefore they are new to it. It has been
known ever since the troglodytes rubbed sticks to
gether and made a fire that it would burn any finger
that is stuck into it, but this knowledge is new to
every child that is born and finds it out by trial.
The club, however, does not stop at its financial
discoveries. It is not content with its childlike faith
that the stamp of the Government can turn fifty'cents'
worth of property into a dollar's worth. It craves for
more new things. Mayor Phelan has these on tap in
the form of public ownership of public utilities. On
Wednesday night he appeared before the club and
exhibited his list, whereupon the club, without leaving
its several seats, amended its constitution so as to call
for Government ownership of telegraphs, telephones,
transportation, wharfage facilities and postal savings
banks, and municipal ownership of water, lighting and
street railroad plants.
One is surprised that the club stopped with this
sumptuous programme. If there is anything neces
sary to human existence it is food, fuel and shelter.
Why chase monopoly out of the freight business, run
it off the wharf and drive* away from the well and
the gas house and leave it to monopolize tenements,
clothing and food supply?
Already the pernicious principle "of the trust has
seized the prune and raisin product of this State, and
henceforth our mince pie is of the humble variety,
eaten in tribute to a trust, and our stewed prunes are
fed to us out of the hand of the monopoly. The wool
and cotton trusts may soon be on our backs,
arjd against whom have reformers said harder things
than the landlord, who criminally erects houses and
wickedly rents them to the poor?
A man may live long and never ride on a street
car, and may nightly retire by the flickering candle
or the moonbeam's misty light. But he must have
some "duds" and a house to live in. One is aston
ished that the progressive Mayor paused in the mid
career of his state communist fight. He should in
clude the real utilities, without which life is impos
sible. Give us state tenement houses, municipal bread
and butter and beefsteak, Government potatoes and
gravy. Let the state -no longer confine its tailor busi
ness to the striped misfits that cover those who serve
it involuntarily and without consideration, but let it
set up as snip to all mankind and womankind, too.
When a lady wants a tailor-made suit' let her file her
plan and specifications with the Secretary* of State
and receive her vestments at the hand's of a Govern
ment messenger boy. who travels over a state rail
road and gets his pay out of the treasury.
The noble Roman-; did as well. The state bought
triticum and triturated it in a state mill and baked
loaves in a state oven, and the grangers all went to
town to feed on state bread, and had nothing to do
but talk politics and mash the heads of each other.
The new Democracy is at Los Angeles far on the
road to the Roman plan. Can it be taken as an ac
cident or shall it be accepted as a sign and portent
that there is also at Los Angeles a general who has
crossed the Rubicon?
Where, the soothsayers? Will not some one bag
and unbowel a gull and study the peristaltic signs of
its interior? The new Democracy is abroad in the
land, and so are other troubles. Where is Zadkiel
and his almanac?
If the story be true that the publication in Germany
of Coghlan's song' about the Kaiser has had the
effect of inclining the people to favor the scheme
of increasing the navy, then our German cousins have
taken the joke entirely too seriously. It was intended
the burden. of the ditty should be a josh and not an
increase of taxation.
When the police have effectively closed the gam
bling rooms that have been running openly, it will be
a good plan to start after those which have been
working on the slyâ but not so very' sly as to be out
of reach of any one wishing to gamble.
The report that the forces of Aguinaldo have been
routed again probably means they have moved out
of the way of our rifles to another hiding-place in the
rice swamps or the jungles and are as much of a fight
ing force as ever.
LETTERS FROM THE PEOPLE.
THE FIBER INDUSTRY IN CALIFORNIA,
To the Editor of The Call: C. P. Huntington, at a recent banquet, parked
that in California we are neglecting many most important things ami in '"*'>
named that of the fiber industry. which in France, Germany, Italy ana
European countries is very highly regarded and cared for. . i_,i_,.
Why is it totally neglected here? Have we no land suitable for it, no die moor
eager for the employment it gives and no capital ready nor citizens enterprises
enough to develop so legitimate and profitable an industry, which, Â«a , *-"S me
world for its market, is capable of almost unlimited expansion? We nave an
these, and feel sure it is only necessary to point the facts out clearly â¢ insure
proper attention by our enterprising capitalists and land owners to this imporwni
matter. . Â . if ,-_
Our farmers now find little profit in the old-time cereal crops, whicn at low
current, prices are scarcely worth growing. They are looking for new ana more
profitable crops to raise, hence the successful attention that is now beginning -o
be given to sugar beets, flax and hemp, ail of which are yearly increasing.
Flax is a good, profitable crop for pur rich, moist lands, easily raised, ana
with recently improved machines bids fair to occupy before long a. prominent
place in our farmers' regard. Its great and almost only drawback is the nana
â¢weeding required to insure its success and that of sugar beets. But no nana
weeding is needed for the growth of flax's twin sister, hemp. This is the most
profitable crop that our -farmers can put into the ground. There is no intensive
farming required, no difficulty in the germination of seed, as in the case or sugar
beets; no expensive hand weeding, as is necessary with flax. ' All our farmers
know, well how to prepare their land and how to seed and cover and. later mow
grain for hay close to the ground. This is exactly the procedure for hemp. =>eiect
moist, loamy landâ island, river bottom or irrigated is best- prepare well as if for
grain-hay, and about April 15 to May 31 sow broadcast fully one to one and a
half bushels per acre of good Japanese hemp seed if possible grown one year
in this State to insure its youth and fecundityâ and nothing more is needed until
90 to 100 days later; the twelve to fifteen feet high stalks are cut down by the
usual mower. The stalks are at present then rotted or "retted" in the field by
the winter's rains, and about March or April are stacked in heaps, dried by the
sun and then decorticated by a very crude machine into 1500 pounds per acre" or
dark, poor hemp "fiber,"- which sells locally to Eastern buyers at about 4 cents
per. pound here free on board cars here.* This pays $60 per acre, of which $30
covers all expenses, having $30 net profit, a. fair result, but handicapped by the
long wait of nine months after harvest before the farmer gets his pay for crop
and labor, as well as by the injury to the quality the hemp fiber by the long
exposure to weather and the defective method of decortication. But all tins is. we'
learn, to be avoided in the near future, as a few public-spirited citizens are
making a most praiseworthy effort to make and put in the field next harvest
some of the latest improved and well vouched for new decorticators. These ma
chines do rapid and effective work in the field the day the hemp stalks are
mowed down, securing one-third more fiber per acre of double the old quality and
sale value, besides saving nine months' delay to the farmer in receiving payment.
This higher quality sells for 8 cents a pound East, and thus nearly doubles the
farmer's profit per acre, which will no doubt largely and rapidly ' increase Our
â¢hemp acreage and our farmers' and land owners' ; prosperity. We therefore be
speak for this movement from our citizens the attention and favor its merits and
benefits deserve, and wish it heartily every success. A. BANNISTER.
TAKES UP THE BROWN MAN'S BURDEN.
To the Editor of The Callâ Dear Sir: I. see by your editorial page that The
Call is the most fearless' paper in San Francisco, and that you are fair enough
to publish letters from. the people whether they voice your sentiments or not.
Therefore I hope you will be kind enough to publish this reply to some editorials
which appeared in the San Francisco Chronicle.
After reading the Chronicle's editorials of April 23 and 27, the former, "An
Object Lesson in Expansion," the latter, ."Little Real Progress," one would think
that the Chronicle is following the advice of R. W. Emerson, who. said, "Say
what you think to-day, even though you contradict what you said yesterday."
To say the least, it is pretty hard to tell which side of the fence the Chronicle
is on. -. â :â¢;.- :
In its* editorial of April 23 it takes our acquisition from Mexico in 1847 as
"An Object Lesson in Expansion" and tries to make it a parallel case 'with
the one which now confronts us. It is" true that our acquisition from Mexico
proved to be a wise policy, but. the Chronicle docs not mention the discovery
of gold, which turned the eyes of the whole world and started the tramping
of tens of thousands of feet in the direction of the Gold Gate, enabling Cali
fornia to step into the sisterhood of States without the previous formation of
a territorial government, thereby becoming the bright-eyed Athena of this
great .republic. .: .
There are in the' Philippines about 1200 islands, aggregating 114 WO square
miles, which is only three-fourths as large as the State of California, and they
now have a population of 10,000,000 half civilized people. Think of it! Take
fourths of California and put 10,000,000 people on it and place 6000 miles
away and then what would be the sole advantage in retaining it? Nothing but
its commerce. There could be no other advantage.
And now (aside from national dignity, for we have already put our foot into
it and to back out Is cowardly) I say is it good policy tor a republic that was
born in the blood of rebellion, and that could never have thrown off the shac
kles of oppression had it not been for the aid of foreign power, is it good policy
I repeat, for a republic that has so dearly learned the blessings of freedom and
self-government to subjugate a poor, down-trodden, tax-eaten and battle-beaten
people who are fighting for self-government and a freedom that is peculiarly
adapted to their own understanding, yea, a subjugation that must come at the
expense of. maintaining an army of 100.000 and may be 300,000 men, a great per
centage of whom will lay down their lives at their post of duty? Yours truly
San Francisco, May 4, 1599. G. T HEALY '
DEWEY DAY AT MOUNT TAMALPAIS,
To the Editor of The Call: There was no trouble or dissatisfaction of any
kind at the Mount Tamalpais Military Academy in connection with the ob
servance of Dewey day. Our headmaster. Mr. Crosby, is an enthusiastic Ameri
can and a strong supporter of the administration and Its policy toward the Phil
ippine Islands. There were excellent reasons which satisfied the hoys as well
as the masters, against taking a whole holiday on that day. Respectfully yours
ALMER MAYO NEWHALL,
ARTHUR W. FOSTER JR.,
CHARLES W. CORBALEY,

THE CRIME IS BATTERYâ B. S., City.
This correspondent asks: "Is it a crime
for a man to force blood from a cut on
his finger down the throat of a young
woman, even though it was claimed to
have been done in fun? If so, what is
the offense?" It is to be presumed that
the act of the man (who by the way
should have been dealt with in a sum
mary manner by some abled-bodied male
relative or friend of the young woman)
was against the will of the young woman. *
That being the case he was guilty of the
crime of battery. A man who would
commit such an act, even though in fun,
as he claimed, should be introduced to
one of the Police Judges by means of a
warrant of arrest.

the dogs met again luring the evening
session the decisions of the afternoon
were reversed. The judge explained the
reason for the reversals by stating that
in the open class he judged the dogs from
a bench show standard (.doubtless â he
judged tln-m according to Stonehenge,
who is a recognized authority on dogs and
the points connected with even well
mart and generally recognized kinds)
and also from a standard that he con
siders applicable to the field. Ashbury,
according to the judge's Idea, is the better
dog on the bench, but in the field he is
an inferior animal *o Alex C.
The question now at variance is what
object has the sportsman In breeding and
raising pointers.' Shall the pointer be
come a bench show dog exclusively or _
dog used for field trial purposes? "if, ac
cording to Judge Lacey; a dog must have
certain qualifications to insure him a blue
or red ribbon on the bench what then is
the use of exhibiting a .field trial winner
if his chances of success are rill? The
judge has certainly cast out a Chinese
puzzle to the pointer men, which will give
them food for thought for some time to
come.
Lacey'*? decisions, so far as the grey
hounds were concerned, were extremely
bad. 01 ing Ski. an imported dog, first
over _;..â¢â  Island Boy and especially vic
tor King, astonished even 'he tyros of the
coursing game. Any man with half an
eye should certainly see that Swedish did
not compare in any way with Systematic,
â¢till Swedish won the blue ribbon. Evi
dently Mr. Lacey Is not well up on the
points of the greyhound. The judging will
be continued to-day and will doubtless
finish on Saturday afternoon.

JUDGE BELCHER AFFIRMS.
THE RIGHT OF BICYCLISTS,
SUSTAINS, THE DECISION OF
THE JUSTICES' COURT.
Drivers Are Held Responsible for Ac
cidents When on the Wrong
Side of the Road.
! Superior Judge Belcher decided a case
: yesterday which is of particular interest
alike to wheelmen and to the users of
other vehicles. The case was an appeal
from the Justices' Court, where judgment
was obtained for the plaintiff. Judge
: Belcher sustained the ruling of the court
! below.
W. T. Macfarlane, a wheelman, was the
plaintiff, being represented by the law
firm of Adams & Adams, who also repre
sent the California Associated Cycling
Clubs in cases of this character. Mr. Mac
farlane, while riding a wheel on Golden
Gate avenue in a westerly direction and
on the right hand side of the road, was
run into by a wagon driven by John
Weber, who was coming down Golden
Gate avenue. Weber was on the wrong
side of the road.
Judge Belcher's opinion was an oral one,
but in deciding the case he reviewed thor
oughly the law of this State and of Can
ada. He held that while it might appear
a hardship in a case which might seem
merely an unavoidable accident. -yet from
the fact that defendant was driving on
the wrong side of the street he must be
held answerable. A person found on that
portion of the highway to Which he has
not the. statutory right must '"' __?'_ an "
swerable for a collision resulting with one
who was on the right side of the thor
Â°The officials of the California Associated
Cycling Clubs are prosecuting vigorously"
all drivers who run down wheelmen while
the latter are riding in the proper place.
RUN OVER AND KILLED.
Steve O'Brien Loses His Life While
Biding on a Freight Train
Near Baden.
Steve O'Brien, who resides at 8 San
' Antonio street, was run over by a freight
train near Baden about 7 o'clock last even
ing and Instantly killed. He was on his
way to see his relative, Robert Ripley,
when he met his untimely death. Imme
diately after the accident his family was.
' notified and his father ami brother drove
! to the scene to recover the body.
The deceased was 30 years old and lived
with his father and brothers. He was a
I bartender by occupation, but was out of
employment. It is thought that he was
I on his way to Baden with a view to ac
â¢ eepting a position. SgBwHPSp
Steve O'Brien was well and popularly
1 known, and his numerous friends will re
| gret to hear of bis sad fate. He leaves a
! fatherland four brothers. The brothers
I are Michael, who is foreman of number 12
] engine; Timothy, who is now in Manila
as a member of the hospital corps, and
Jerry and George.

Rally and Essay Contest.
The League of the Cross will hold a
rally and essay contest in Metropolitan
Hall next Sunday afternoon. Great- in
terest Is centered in the contest, as it is
the last of the present series, which has
been so [successful. The winner will not
only be awarded a silver medal but will
have the privilege of competing for the
Archbishop Riordan medal in August.
Five parishes- St. Mary's Cathedral,
Sacred Heart, Holy Cross, St. Francis
and St. Brigld'sâ will be represented. Rev.
James McDonald of St. Peter's. Church
will deliver the address. An excellent mu
sical programme, including selections by
the glee club of Company A, will be fur
nished. Admission will be free to all.

CHRISTIAN ENDEAVORERS'
QUARTERLY CONVENTION
INTERESTING SESSION OF GOLD
EN GATE UNION.
Exercises in the First Presbyterian
Church Drs. Mackenzie, Dille
and Coyle Deliver Ad
dresses.
The forty-ninth quarterly convention of i
Golden Gate Christian Endeavorers was
held in the First Presbyterian Church,
corner of Van Ness avenue and Sacra
mento street, last night. C. M. Whitney
presiding. The church was well filled,
and the proceedings were decidedly inter
esting. Addresses were made by Dr. Mac
kenzie, pastor of the church in which the
convention was held; 1 >rs. Dllle and Coyle, \
and George R. Lowell of Oakland.
The proceeding- were opened by .-i praise
service, conducted by Professor Fleisch
ner and orchestra, followed by devotional
exercises led by 11. 11. Bell, D.D. The
routine business of the convention was
then taken up. It consisted of reports of
the various committees and occupied but
a few minutes. Mr. Lowell delivered a
brief address on the preparations for the
holding of the State convention at Oak
land during the present year, and after a
solo by J. George Gibson. D.D., Dr. Dille
was Introduced to speak on "Personal
Responsibility In Christian Endeavor
Work." . The Oakland divine's remarks
were directed to pointing out to the young
toll's the necessity of continued and well
directed effort in the field of Christian
endeavor, and be urged them to not over
look the fact that the results of their la
bors depended on the personal effort put
into the work which they had volunteered
to perform. Dr. Coyle' s address on the
"Missionary Spirit in <_'. E. Work" was
along the same lines. He called attention
to the fact that Christ was the greatest
missionary of all, and said the example
furnished "by him should be an inspiration
to all those who believed in and were en
gaged in promulgating his teachings.
"The Battle Hymn of Missions ' was
then sung, by Rev. Mr. Gibson, the entire
convention joining in singing the chorus
standing, and the programme was con
cluded by Dr. Mackenzie, who spoke upon
the efficacy of prayer in Christian En
deavor work. At the close of his address
the pastor dismissed the gathering with
a benediction.

COMMERCIAL WORLD.
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver declined.
Mexican liars lower.
Wheat .lull and unchanged.
Barley futures advanced.
Oats, Corn and Rye unchanged.
Hay weak and In large receipt.
Feedstuff s unchanged
Beans and Seeds dull.
Potatoes' and Onions weak.
Vegetables steady to firm.
Poultry du'l and weak.
Butter and Cheese about the same.
Eggs doing rather better.
Fruit more or leas scarce and firm.
Some damage to Raisins by frost.
Hams active and advancing.
Fair demand for Wool.
Hogs still scarce and firm.
Mutton and Lamb lower.
Beef has a wider range.
Pig Tin and Lead higher.
Sugar firm.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
(RECAST.
The pressure has fallen steadily along the
northern coast, and while there is no well
marked depression and little likelihood of heavy
rain, the conditions favor light showers with i
the clouds soon breaking away along the coast I
from Mendocino northward, and possibly light
showers in the northern portion of California
on Friday morning.
The temperature has risen over all the
country west of the Rocks Mountains, except
along the California coast from San Francisco
southward to San Diego, where* probably be
cause "i fog there has been. a slight fall. The
temperatures are more nearly normal In Cali
fornia than they have been for some time pre
vious.
There has been no rainfall in the State during
the past twenty-four hours.
Forecast made at San Francisco for 30 hour
ending midnight May *,:
Northern Californiaâ Fair Friday, with fog
along the coast In the morning, and also in the
Sacramento Valley; west winds on coast; south
winds inland.
Southern Californiaâ Fair Friday; west winds, i
Nevadaâ Cloudy Friday.
Arizonaâ Fair Friday.
San Francisco and vicinity- Fair Friday,
with fog in the morning; warmer, with south
erly changing to westerly winds.
ALEXANDER McADIE, Forecast Official.
EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. May 4.â The stock market was (
weak most Of the day. then rallied, recovering
part of the earlier loss and left off 'with an \
uncertain tone, with net losses of a substan
tial fraction prevailing. Unfounded rumors
were rife, including stories of a split between
prominent bull interests, the death of a promi
nent banker and statements that banks had;
called loans and refused further accommoda
tions to a large Interest. Each of these rumors
In turn was positively denied, but not until
substantial declines in stocks had been effected,
London was a liberal selier. Sugar was most
prominent in the period of 'depression, drop
ping over 9 points, two-thirds of which â¢ was
afterward recovered. The rest of the market
followed the fluctuations in sugar to a minor
extent. The iron and steel stocks, local trans
portation shares. Anaconda and a number of
the other specialties made an average supreme
drop of about 8 points, while there w.ere nu- ;
merous declines of about 1 point. With the
rally In sugar substantial recoveries occurred
elsewhere, particularly in those which were
most depressed previously, and the market
thereafter became dull. Consolidated '".as was
well taken and rose over 3 points. Among the
railways Burlington, which sells ex-rights to
morrow, displayed the most strength. There
were fractional, advances In Continental To- |
bacco, Tennessee Coal and Iron and General j
Electric. The most prominent net declines I
were in New York Central, the coalers, local
traction companies, ron and steel shares and I
most of the grangers. There were many of the
opinion that heavy liquidation for me impor
tant interests occurred to-day and that much
of the latti-r's strength came from the cover
ing of shorts. An Interest which it was re
ported was seeking to reduce its line In its
specialties denied that it had any speculative
ventures on foot just now. Commission house
business was said in be the smallest for some
time.
Railroad bonds showed a reactionary tend
ency for the greater part of the day, but later,
owing to conspicuous strength in Wisconsin
Central firsts and support to other speculative
lssuis, imparted a better tone. Total sales,
$8,300,000.
United States 3's coupon and the new 4's cou
pon declined %. but the s's coupon advanced
l Â« In the bid price.

NEW YORK, May 4.â The Commercial Ad
' vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
j The markets here were dull and uneasy to-
I day. The Transvaal apprehension was re
! vived on vague talk of an ultimatum. 1 don't
. credit the reports.
Americans were heavy all day. Liverpool
! liquidated heavily early. The close was heavy
i at bottom prices. Tlntos were 18%; Anacondas,
- 12%; Bostons, 2 9-16.
Silver was quiet and dull. There were no
i American sales. Money was plentiful. Thirty
nine thousand pounds sterling gold In bars ,
was bought by the bank.

BRADSTREET'S ON TRADE CONDITIONS.
NEW YORK, May 4.â Bradstreet's of May 6
will say: Widespread business activity and
heavy financial transactions, resulting from or
consequent upon the large number of Industrial
combinations planned or executed during the
month of April, seem to have been largely
responsible for the heavy bank clearings re
ported for that month, because stock specula
tion was quieter than in March or the pre
ceding months. The April clearings, in fact,
deserve special notice, because though they
i were smaller by 5 per cent than the aggregate
for March, this was owing chiefly to the smaller
: number of business 'lays in that month, because
i the average dally clearings were heavier than
; the like averase for the nionth of March. In
percentage gain. too, the increase shown over
April a year ago was the heaviest noted this
year, and the result is a further enlargement
of the gain reported for the calendar year over
recent i 'receding periods.
The aggregate bank clearings for the month
just closed. were 38,266,. a gain of 65.5 per
cent over April 1 a year ago, an' increase of 102
pec cent over the month of April, 1897, a gain
of 122 per cent over the corresponding month
in 1894, .'and an increase of 63 per cent over the
like period of 1892. The heavy gain for April :
compares with an Increase of 54.6 per cent dur- I
j ing the month of March, of 25.7 per cent In I
February and of 41.3 per cent in January over
corresponding periods of last Â«year.
Relatively the best showing for April, as In- !
. deed for many preceding months, was that i
1 made by the Middle States group of cities. In
this section the increase reported over April
a year ago was in excess of 89 per cent, the
next heaviest gain being that reported by the
New England cities, 4-* per cent.
Other considerable gains are. those of 23 per
cent In the Southern cities, 13.7 in the Western
group, and 11.3 In the Northwestern. As for
some time past, the gains in the Southwest
: and the far Western groups have been com- '
paratively moderate, but even here the Increase
have ranged from 6 to 8 per cent.
a rather better showing is naturally made

per cent, against 27 per cent in March. In the

cago shows a gain of 14.5 per cent over a year

LONDON WOOL SALES.
LONDON. May ...âThe offerings at the wool
auction sales to-day were largely merinos, and
sold in sellers' favor, particularly low me
dium crossbreds, which were taken principally
to Yorkshire. Greasy merinos showed an ad
vance of 10% per cent, the highest average
reached in many years, and due largely to the
determination of the Continental buyers to
secure this Stock. Good Hope and Natal wools
were eagerly sought after* ana Puma Arenas
in good condition ruled firm, fine selling at 34d
and coarse %d higher. The majority of this
stock was taken by the Continent. The usual
large attendance was -present:' and the number
of bales offered numbered 14,380.

DECIDUOUS AND CITRUS FRUITS.
Receipts were 700 boxes Cherries and 644
chests Strawberries.
A good many adverse reports regarding the '
fruit crop have been received this week, and i
there considerable talk over the matter on I
the street yesterday. The commission merchants
are practically agreed that with the single ex
ception of Peaches the crop of orchard fruits
will be light this year, and they look for good
prices and an active trade in consequence.
Strawberries are the weakest fruit on the
list. Cherries and Gooseberries are steady at
about previous prices. The few Apricots that
come about half a dozen crates a dayâ sell
very slowly, as they are small and inferior.
Citrus Fruits are quiet. Oranges are in mod
; crate and decreasing supply.

HIDES. TALLOW, WOOL AND HOPS.
' A local firm says of Wool: "There is a fair
trade for good free shipping Wools, and picked
lots find ready sale at full figures. There Is
more inquiry also for Inferior scouring fall
Wools, but at unchanged rates. The better
local demand is incident to the stronger mar
ket opening of the London Wool sales the first
of this Week."

SAN FRANCISCO MEAT MARKET.
Beef is easy and prices have a wider range.
Mutton and Lamb are lower. Hogs continue
scarce and. firm, but prices are no higher,
though It Is reported that several houses who
were short of stock have paid up to 6c.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographtc
Office, located In the Merchants' Exchange, la
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense. ;.. -v.
Navigators are cordially invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and . all matters
of interest to ocean commerce. ; '-*â.'
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building at the foot of Market street. Is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Naval
Observatory. Mare Island. Cal.
A notice stating whether the hall was dropped
on time or giving the error. If any. Is published
in the morning papers the following day.
C. G. CALKIN*?.
Lieutenant. I*. S. N.. In charge.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third tide
. and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
! given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
1 when a minus sign ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
; the depth given by the charts. The plane of
â¢ reference Is the mean of the lower low waters.

Reopening Day of the Pacific Coast
Jockey Flimsy Sport
That Failed to Please.
Devereux Backed.
The exiled Pacific Coast Jockey Club
now holds possession of the Oakland race
track. Yesterday the Ingleside associa
tion commenced what will be the closing
meeting of the racing season. Its at
tendance was not large, and if half as
ninny people are present this afternoon
many smart ones will miss their guess.
A morsel of turf history will explain it
all.
One day during the preceding meeting
of the club named Pat Morrissey won a
race and was disqualified in favor of the
Corrlgan entry, Adolph Spreckels. It was
a selling affair and Corrigan's horse 'Â»--*
given a substantial "boost" by "Butch"
Fisher, a well-known trainer and owner.
A few days later Fisher captured a race
with Pot^nte, after which the Hawthorne
turfman stepped nut and, bidding up the
winner, secured the animal. This was tit
for tat, and the brace of turfmen shook
hands. It was not a Masonic shake,
though. Yesterday Fisher started a re
cent purchase of his. Dare 11. in the third
race, and the- horse won in a big gallop.
Personne finishing second. rrigan fur
nished the 3 to 10 favorite Sardonic, which
ran fourth. At the stretch turn Dai was
pinched on the rail, which imping was
caused by "Colonel Roosevelt" Pig
gott on the favorite and Macklin,
with the le_r up on Red Glenn,
a "lobster" by the looks of the
betting. Jenkins took his mount up, and
traveling around his field won as before
mentioned. After weighing In Glover, the
rider of Personne, was called Into the
stand by Judge Smith, and then Jenkins
was surhmoned before the tribunal. Pa
trol Judpe Moore was next questioned and
the doom of Dare \va.s read on their
faces. Fisher had bearded the lion In his
den and must suffer. Dare was disquali
fied amid the hoots and howls of the dis
gusted spectators. It might also be men
tioned that Personne was backed from 20
down to 10 and 12.
The card throughout was a ragged
looking one, permitting of four lopsided
favorites winning.
A carefuliy planned coup was executed
in the two-year-old event. â  vereux . a
colt by Puryear 1 rizelda, with Jenkins â¢
on his back, and backed from 20 down to
7 10.1. won imposingly from a big field of
youngsters. Always in front, he finished
three lengths ahead of Tanobe at the
wire. Armistice, the 4 to 5 favorite, rid
den by Piggott, ran unplaced.
With Pelxotto scratched from the intro
ductory event, a mile selling run, Social
ist, the mount of Jenkins, closed favorite.
The bay gelding led from the start, win
ning in a walk from Judge uffer. The i

Qolota took the final run without effort
from Imp. Mistral.
Track Notes.
Jenkins distinguished himself by put
ting three winners over the plate. The
promising rider has supplanted Bullman
In the public's affections. He was fined
$50 for his ride on Dare.
A well-known young turfman from Los
Angeles was credited with taking over
$5000 out of the ring over the victory of
: Devereux.

In the Divorce Court.
Decrees of divorce have been granted
Ella Clements from Fred Clements on
the ground of failure to provide; Roletta
S. Gussander from Fritz A. Gussander on
the ground of extreme cruelty, and
Eruda Gulda from Santino Gulda on the
ground that the defendant had. been con
victed of a felony. Suits for divorce have
been filed by Mary E. Hitchcock against
William H. Hitchcock for failure to pro
vide: Mina F. Dutree against Jose Dutree
for- cruelty and Emma M. Anderson
against Oscar Anderson for desertion.
TUB BAN FKAKCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, MAY 5, 1899.
AN AGED TEACHER
FINALLY RETIRES
Close of a Career of a Quarter Century
in College of Pharmacy.
WILLIAM T. WENZELL, Emeritus Professor.
AFTER an honorable career of twenty-seven years a? professor of chem
istry in the Pharmaceutical Colege, William T. Wenzell has resigned.
His threescore y< ars ami ten rest heavily on the educator, and he has
retired to his ranch at Napa, where he will spend the waning years of
his life. As a token of appreciation of his ability as an instructor and his
long connection with tho College of Pharmacy, he was made emeritus pro
fessor. This is purely an honorary title, conferred in recognition of con
scientious and unremitting labor.
The dean of tho college, William M. Searby, speaks highly of Professor
Wenzell. "He is one of the oldest and most highly respected Instructors on
the Pacific Coast," Bays Mr. Searby. "Last year he suffered a severe at
tack of la grippe, which lowered his vitality and deprived him of that
evenness of temper bo necessary in an instructor."
Back of the resignation of Professor Wenzell is a story of the disaffection
of the'junior class at the College of Pharmacy and of friction between the
professor and his pupils. A meeting was held in November last to deter
mine whether, the students Bhould continue their year's work in chemistry,
or, on the other hand, request the faculty to provide another instructor.
The pupils claimed that advancing years had so reduced the efficiency of
their professor that he was unable to cope with forty active, energetic
students. The matter was presented to the faculty and the students under
stood that another lecturer would be provided. This was not done, how
ever, and tli-' class took matters Into their own hands.
The- entry of Professor Wenzell to the classroom was invariably thÂ»> sig
nal for a noisy demonstration, making the delivery of a lecture Impossible.
When the examination in chemistry was held last week not a student ap
peared either in the classroom or on the college grounds. Professor Wen
zell resigned at once. 'Die faculty conferred upon him the title of Emeri
tus professor as recognition of his splendid work for the college during the
quarter of a century he has been one of its foremost figures. i

ALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.
AN EXPLOSION
THAT MARKS A
NEW DEPARTURE
No Loss of Life at
Pinole.
â * â
MIXING HOUSE WRECKED
â â â¦
WARNED EMPLOYES ELED IN
ALL DIRECTIONS.
â¦
A Minute After Their Departure the
Place Where They Had Been
Working Was Annihilated
by Dynamite.
â¦
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
908 Broadway, May 4.
When Foreman Wunderliek detected the
fumes of something burning in the mix
ing house at the California Powder Works
to-day lie at once gave the order to the
ten Chinese employes to run away. They
did not hesitate a second, but ran like
hares and saved their lives. Before they
had fairly reached a place of safety a
terrific explosion threw some of them to
the ground and the valuable plant and
mixing house was instantly reduced to a
heap of smoking ruins.
A few minutes after 9 o'clock this morn
ing the sound as of thunder coming from
the bay shore north of Oakland pro
claimed another explosion. People are so
familiar with this sound that no one asks
the cause of it, but there is an immediate
rush to -i. -phones to inquire how many
lives have been lost.
This morning's accident was one of the
most wonderful on record, for although
the damage was very great not a single
life was -:. and the total Injury to hu
man beings was a few scratches on a la
borer's head. In this regard the explosion
has made a record.
When J. Wunderllck, the foreman, was |
In the mixing-house his practiced nostrils
detected the odor nt burning wood pulp,
which is used in the manufacture of dyna
mite. He spent but a few seconds trying
to determine the cause of the smoke, and
then yelled to the Chinese to fly as fast )
us their legs could carry them. Wunder
lick says he doesn't remember just what
he said, but he believes that no Apache
whoop could equal the yell he gave warn-
Ing the Chinese to flee. They had gone
several hundred yards when the place, in j
the language of powder works, "went !
up." The name which the foreman had j
seen In the main mixing-room had reached
a tank of explosive material and a second
later many of ;he Hying Chinese were j
knocked off their feet and others were
dodging clouds of living fragments.
Chris Fass, a laborer, was driving a car ;
on the track leading from the mixing- i
house to the entrance to the works. The
concussion was so great that he was
thrown from the car and struck by flying |
splinters, which scratched his head, but (
Inflicted no serious injury.
The wreck is very complete, but the
daman-' will not exceed $6000. A few min
utes Lfter the explosion the employes sub
dued some flames which had spread to ad
joining sheds and then proceeded to clear
away the mass of splintered lumber, |
tanks, broken machinery and maze of j
pipework. There is never any danger In
the ruins of an explosion at the powder!
works, for every one knows that nothing
explosive could possibly be found within
any reasonable distance of a mixing- |
house that has gone up. The building j
was erected a few months ago and was a :
very elaborate plant. It took the place !
of a mixing-house that went up over a â¢
year ago. The company has a duplicate
dynamite plant. and Superintendent
Bermlngham says that In a few days work
will be (â â int.- "ii as though there had been
no explosion this morning.
UNITARIANS CLOSE
THEIR ANNUAL MEET
HON. HORACE DAVIS IS ELECTED
PRESIDENT.
Women's Unitarian Conference of the
Pacific Coast Will Meet in Oak
land To-Day.
Oakland Office San Francisco < 'all,
908 Broadway, May 4.
The concluding session of the Pacific
Unitarian Conference was held in the
First Unitarian Church this afternoon
and was well attended. Judge Works
presided and the devotional exercises
this morning were led by Rev. W. E.
Copeland. Addresses followed by F. I.
Bamford, Dr. Dorothea Moore, Arthur B.
Ellis and Rev. J. T. Sunderland.
At this afternoon's session Rev G. li
Dodson of Alameda delivered an excep
tionally aide discourse on the subject "To
Lead In the Adjustment of Religious
Thought to the Doctrine of Evolution "
At Its conclusion Rev. Dodson was so
vigorously applauded that he was com
pelled through courtesy to resume the
platform and talk ten minutes longer.
Rev. George T. Weaver next spoke on
"The Making of Christianity Really
Christian. A brief business session fol
lowed, during which Hon. Horace Davis
was elected president for th.- ensuing
year.
To-morrow morning the Women's Uni
tarian Conference of the Pacific Coast
will meet. Miss Elizabeth B. Easton
president, will deliver the address of wel
come, and the speakers of the day will
be Mrs. R. Frank Clark of Alameda
Anna E. Pratt, Mrs. John Rush Dr
Alida C. Avery of San Jose. Rev Sarah
Pratt of Seattle aim Mrs. .1. T. Sunder
land of Oakland.

Seamstress Wants Damages.
OAKLAND, May 4.â Anne Jepson filed
suit In the Superior Court this afternoon
against the Oakland Transit Company for
the recovery of $5000 damages. She Al
leges that she was violently thrown from
an electric car at Fourth and Prospect
avenues, on November 14 last, through
the carelessness of the motorman in
starting the car before she had properly
boarded, sustaining injuries that nave
disabled her from earning a livelihood at
sewing and housework.
NO MORE JOSHES IN
THE BLUE AND GOLD
BERKELEY, .May Prompted by
the tone of the last Blue and
Gold, recently Issued by the ju
nior class of the University of
California, the members of the faculty
have decided that henceforth no
more lampooning and caricaturing of
professors and students will be al
lowed in the college annual. In addi
tion to this they have followed up
their action of a week ago in dismiss
ing from college the editor-in-chief,
Stuart A. Masters by meting out the
same discipline to the manager.
George Brehm. A notice was posted
on the bulletin board to-day by order
of the Academic Council dismissing
Brehm from the university.
Brehm undertook the work of get
ting out the Blue and Gold in con
junction with Masters shortly after
the beginning of last term. The two
have worked indefatigably'nt the pub
lication for several months past, striv
ing to outdo all their predecessors both
it: the extent of the book and in the
originality of its "joshes." They
succeeded in getting out the largest
annual which has ever been issued
at liege, but, according to the views
of the faculty, their originality went
a little too far and so the professors
have concluded once and for all that no college editor is to indulge any
more in indiscriminate Joshing.
The notice announcing the action of the Academic Council with regard to
Brehm was prepared by a committee consisting of Professors George H.
Howison, E. W. Eiilgard and W. 'â :. Bitter. it reads In part as follows:
The university cannot allow itself to lie discredited by. any unseemly publico.!
purporting to represent the university life and spirit. - /
The recent Issue of "The Blue and Gold" is unseemly; in its Insults to fellow
students, especially to the young women; in its personal attacks on the university au
thorities, Including the president of the board of regents; in the low and coarse char
acter of certain pictures, jests and Innuendoes.
The president ami the committee think it reasonable, and rule:
That no student publication can l.- tolerated which tends to discredit the univer
sity In the community at large and in the sisterhood of reputable universities. Of
this tendency the council is to be the judge.
That the so-called "josh" element, used for ridicule, slur or personal attack
tending to bring its objects Into contempt or disrespect, must in the future be omit
ted from all student publications.
That nothing 'an be permitted which verges on coarseness or Indecency.
That Manager Brehm. who admits ins equal responsibility with the chief editor
for the objectionable features of the present Issue of "The Blue and Gold," be, and
hereby if, dismissed from the university.
That all the staff of "The Blue and Gold," both editorial and Managing, be, and
hereby arc, publicly reprimanded for culpable negligence In committing themselves
blindly to such leadership.
For the Academic Council: MARTIN KELLOGG, President.
The announcement was not posted until very late this afternoon, and as
the students are In the midst of final examinations for the year, some of
them having already left the college town, it is doubtful as to how the ruling
of the faculty will be received. The petition recently presented in behalf of
Masters, In which over 1000 students asked to have him reinstated, may serve
as an Index to the opinion of the undergraduates in the matter. It is more
than likely that 'hey will be as unanimous in asking to have Brehm reinstat
ed. The Impression prevails that both Brehm and Masters have met more
than their just deserts, for while it is generally admitted that both were
indiscreet, the whole undergraduate body takes exception to the severity of
the faculty's discipline.
NEW CONTRACT FOR
WORK ON LAP MERRITT
BOARD OF WORKS NEEDS NO
FURTHER AUTHORITY.
None of the Money Raised for Im
provements Can Be Legally
Used for Any Other
Purpose.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call, j
908 Broadway. May I.
Within a few days a new contract will
be let for carrying on tin- Improvements
In and around Lake Merritt. The Board
of Works holds that it has no need of any i
further advice from the City Council, as j
the ordinance authorizing the work is -:i
effect and the courts having annulled th" j
contract therefore no contract has been
let and the board must proceed with all
possible diligence.
In view of the fact that the contractors i
through n<> fault of their own are in .1
position yvhere they are likely to lose a :
large sum of money there Is a general i
desire to award the new contract to tin m j
if possible. Of course it must be awarded i
under the law to the lowest bidder, but '
I'cterson and Johnson, the contractors, |
hay* already built their dredger and have
all the lumber on the scene and have done j
considerable work. Of course the i>r .- I
posals will be for all th.- work to be d' ne,
and it is argued that it will be Impossible I
for any other contractor unless he be
willfully anxious to lose money, to come j
in at this stage and bid as advantages I
as the contractor- who have already 1
made such a good start.
When asked about the matter to-day
Mayor Snow said that he does not believe j
any one wants to see contractors suffer
because of a technical error in their con
tract. "So far as 1 can learn." said Mr.
Snow, "there is no complaint whatever
regarding the manner in which the work
has been done, and the city should pay
for it. There may have to be some relax
ation of strict law in the matter, but
there i.^ also such a thing as equity, and
good work having been performed. I
would be very sorry to Bee the contractors
required to lose many thousands of dol
lars."

DR. ANDERSON OBEYS
THE SIGNATURE LAW
WRITES THAT HE IS NOT THE
CHARMER OF MRS. BOSCOW. " I
Says Boscow Is an Ingrate Who Had
His Benefactor Thrown Into
a Dungeon.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
908 Broadway, May i.
Dr. 11. IV. Anderson has Issued a state
ment over his signature, In which he se-
riously arraigns W. A. Boscow. the man
who Anderson claims secured the doctor's
arrest for illegally practicing. The story
told by the doctor is interesting and ex
ceedingly romantic.
Boscow has a charming wife, but at
present their relations are somewhat
strained. Mrs. Boscow Is inclined to de
fend Dr. Anderson.
Dr. Anderson, who will soon be tried
under the ordinance prohibiting doctors
from practicing without a license, de
clares that Boscow believes him guilty
of many Imaginary wrongs, and has
vented his spite by having him cast into
prison.
"After 1 had done my utmost to pro
mote his health and happiness, never re
fusing to go to his assistance* and work
ing with nim during his sickness many
dreary nights, for which I never charged
him one cent, this Christian man re
turns my kindness by having mi- thrown
into a dungeon, by bringing sorrow and
desolation to the heart .-.- my invalid wife
by accusing me of bavin- broken- up ins
home, when my wife and I have fre
quently advised his wife to remain with
her husband, if for no other reason than
for the sake of her little children," is a
portion of Dr. Anderson's defense.
W. A. Bobcow declares that Anderson
hypnotized his wife while they were liv
ing in the same house and Induced her
to leave him. Mrs. Boscow denies this
and proclaims that her husband's action
in causing Anderson's arrest is ror the
purpose of trying to connect ids name
with the divorce proceedings and to sub
ject htm to shame and dishonor.
Anderson protests that he is able to
clear himself of th.- charge and promises
to pursue Boscow to the bitter end.

Dunlea, residing In Connecticut, Id) acres
if land near Altamont. Should he be dead
W. E. Hinz.
or not found within three years after tes
tatrix's death this land is to go to her
aunt, Margaret Hennessey of Queensland,
Australia, and in the event of the latter's
death, or should she not Â«-c found within
the next six years the property is left to

Australia. Money on deposit in the Hi
bernia Bank. San Francisco, and Bank of
